Refutation of the Hashwiyya on God's Speech
Refutation of Hashwiyya on God's speech as letters and sounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Hashwiyya/Hanabila belief about God's speech and why it is condemned as a composite of errors from other religions.

Core Idea
The Hashwiyya view that God's speech is eternal letters and sounds is an extreme error.

The passage strongly criticizes those who claim God's speech is composed of eternal letters and sounds. Al-Sanusi's note analyzes this belief as a combination of Jewish, Christian, and Mu'tazilite errors. This lesson unpacks that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Hashwiyya claim
Read the Arabic passage stating the Hashwiyya view: 'كلامه تعالى هو الحروف والأصوات المتوالية المترتبة ويزعمون أنها قديمة'.
2
Identify the three misguidances
In the footnote, al-Sanusi lists three origins: Judaism (body), Christianity (indwelling of speech), and Mu'tazilism (letters and sounds).
3
Understand the critique
The Hashwiyya are condemned for extreme literalism, even claiming the Quran's cover is eternal.
4
Compare with the Sunni position
Reflect on the stark difference: Sunni speech is non-composite and eternal; Hashwiyya speech is composite and temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٥. صفة الكلام ودليلها]
قوله : (كَذَا الكَلامُ ) ( كذا) خبرٌ ،مقدَّم ، و (الكلام) مبتدأ مؤخر، والمعنى: الكلام مثل ذا ؛ أي مثل ما تقدم من الصفات والتشبيه ليس من كل وجه بل في مطلق الوجوب الله تعالى وإن خالفها في الدليل؛ لأنَّ دليلها عقلي؛ إما وحده، وإما مع النقلي على وجه التأكيد، ودليله نقلي إما وحده أو مع العقلي(١) على وجه التأكيد؛ فالمعول عليه فيه الدليل السمعي كما سيذكره بقوله : (بذي أتانا السمع). وقد اختلف أهل الملل والمذاهب في معنى كلامه تعالى، فقال أهل السنة : صفةٌ أزليةٌ قائمة بذاته تعالى ليست بحرف ولا صوت منزهة عن التقدم والتأخر والإعراب والبناء، ومنزهة عن السكوت النفسي بألا يدبر في نفسه الكلام مع القدرة عليه، ومنزهةً عن الآفة الباطنية بألا يقدر على ذلك والطفولية. ا في حال الخرس وقالت الحشوية وطائفةٌ سموا أنفسهم بالحنابلة كلامه تعالى هو الحروف والأصواتُ المتوالية المترتبةُ ، ويزعمون أنها قديمة، وتغالى بعضُهم حتى زعم قدم هذه الحروف التي نقرؤها والرسوم، بل تجاوز جهلُ بعضهم لغلافِ المصحف (٣) .
(١) قوله : (إما وحده أو مع العقلي معناه أنك بالخيار بين أن تستدل بالدليل النقلي وتقتصر عليه، وبين أن تضم إليه الدليل العقلي للتأكيد والتقوية، وهو ما ذكره السنوسي بقوله: (وأيضاً لو لم يتصف بها لزم أن يتصف بأضدادها وهي نقائص والنقص عليه تعالى محال). انتهى أجهوري.
(٢) قوله : (كما في حال الخرس إلخ) التشبيه في مطلق الآفة، وإن كانت الآفة المشبهة هي الآفة الباطنية المانعة من الكلام النفسي والآفة المشبهة بها وهي الخرس، والطفولية ظاهرية مانعة من الكلام اللفظي . انتهى أجهوري.
(٣) قال الإمام السنوسي في شرح الكبرى ( ص ٢٦٧) : (وبالجملة فاعتقاد الحشوية تألف من ضلالات ثلاث : : من تهود وتنصُّر واعتزال ؛ فهم مع اليهود في اعتقادهم الجسم في حق الله، ومع النصارى في اعتقاد حلول الكلام في الأجسام، وأنه لا يفارق مع ذلك الإله، ومع المعتزلة في اعتقاد أن كلام الله تعالى حروف وأصوات، وهو نص مذهب اليهود أيضاً، غير أن المعتزلة لم يقولوا بقيام الحروف والأصوات به سبحانه وتعالى لما تفطنوا لحدوثها، وأدركوا أن قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى محال، وهؤلاء حكموا بذلك لعظيم غباوتهم وجهلهم الضروريات التي تدرك بأوائل العقول، واشترك الجميع في عدم تعقل ما قاله أهل الحق من إثبات كلام ليس بحرف ولا صوت قائم بنفس المتكلم، يعبر عنه بالكلام اللفظي والكتابة والرموز والإشارات).
[5. The Attribute of Speech and Its Proof]
His statement: (Thus speech) – (Thus) is a predicate placed before, and (speech) is the subject placed after, and the meaning is: speech is like this; that is, like the preceding attributes, and the comparison is not in every respect but in the absolute obligatoriness for God, exalted, even if it differs from them in the proof. Because their proof is rational, either alone or with the transmitted as an emphasis, and its proof is transmitted either alone or with the rational as an emphasis; so reliance is placed upon the transmitted proof as he will mention by his saying: (With which the hearing brought us). And the people of religions and sects differed concerning the meaning of His, exalted, speech. The People of the Sunna said: an eternal attribute subsisting in His, exalted, essence, not a letter or a sound, transcendent of anteriority and posteriority, inflection and declension, and transcendent of psychological silence such that He does not deliberate speech in Himself while being able to do so, and transcendent of the internal defect such that He is not able to do that and childishness in the case of dumbness. And the Hashwiyya and a group who call themselves Hanabila said: His, exalted, speech is the consecutive ordered letters and sounds, and they claim they are eternal, and some of them exaggerated until they claimed the eternity of these letters we recite and the scripts, rather the ignorance of some of them exceeded to the cover of the Quran. (1) His statement: (either alone or with the rational) means that you have the choice between deducing with the transmitted proof and limiting yourself to it, and between adding to it the rational proof for emphasis and strengthening, and this is what al-Sanusi mentioned in his saying: (Also, if He were not described with it, He would have to be described with their opposites, which are deficiencies, and deficiency is impossible for Him, exalted). End of al-Ujhuri. (2) His statement: (as in the case of dumbness etc.) the comparison is in the absolute defect, although the defect compared is the internal defect preventing psychological speech, and that which it is compared to is dumbness, and childishness is external preventing verbal speech. End of al-Ujhuri. (3) Imam al-Sanusi said in his commentary on al-Kubra (p. 267): (In summary, the belief of the Hashwiyya is composed of three misguidances: from Judaism, Christianity, and Mu'tazilism. They are with the Jews in their belief in the body for God, with the Christians in their belief in the indwelling of speech in bodies and that it does not separate from the divine, and with the Mu'tazila in their belief that the speech of God, exalted, is letters and sounds, and this is also the explicit doctrine of the Jews, except that the Mu'tazila did not say that letters and sounds subsist in Him, exalted, because they realized they are originated and understood that the subsistence of originated things in His essence is impossible. These people judged this due to their great stupidity and ignorance of necessary things perceived by primary intellects. And all shared in not comprehending what the People of Truth said of affirming speech that is not a letter nor a sound, subsisting in the speaker, expressed by verbal speech, writing, symbols, and indications.)
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of the poem about the attribute of speech. It begins by explaining the grammar of the word 'speech' in the text. The comparison of speech to the preceding attributes is not in every respect but in the absolute necessity of the attribute for God. The proof for speech is primarily transmitted, though a rational proof can be added for emphasis. Then the passage presents the Sunni doctrine: God's speech is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, not composed of letters or sounds, transcendent of temporal features and defects. It then contrasts this with the views of the Hashwiyya and self-proclaimed Hanabila, who believe God's speech is literal letters and sounds that are eternal, some even claiming the letters we recite and the physical Quran cover are eternal. The footnotes elaborate: the rational proof for speech (that denying it implies deficiencies) comes from Sanusi; the comparison with dumbness is explained; and a longer footnote from Sanusi's Sharh al-Kubra classifies the Hashwiyya belief as a combination of Jewish, Christian, and Mu'tazilite errors, noting that the Mu'tazila at least avoided attributing originated letters to God, unlike the Hashwiyya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the Hanabila (or Hashwiyya) are correct in asserting that God's speech is literal letters and sounds.
Correction: The text labels this as a combination of heresies and emphasizes that God's speech is not composed of letters or sounds.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sanusi, what are the three misguidances that form the Hashwiyya belief?
A) Judaism, Christianity, and Zoroastrianism
B) Judaism, Christianity, and Mu'tazilism
C) Christianity, Mu'tazilism, and Atheism
D) Judaism, Mu'tazilism, and Sufism

Key Takeaway
The Hashwiyya belief that God's speech is eternal letters and sounds is a compounded error rooted in earlier heresies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined God's speech as something you could hear with your ears, like a human voice? How does this lesson challenge that assumption, and what does it demand you rethink about the nature of your Creator?
?
The Hashwiyya borrowed errors from Jews, Christians, and Mu'tazila, combining them into an extreme belief. Is there any area of your own faith where you have unknowingly mixed ideas from different sources without checking them against sound theology? Will you take concrete steps to verify your beliefs?
?
If God's speech is not composed of letters and sounds, then what does it mean for the Quran to be His word? How does this truth change the way you approach recitation and reflection? Commit to one change in your relationship with the Quran based on this lesson.

Verbal and Internal Speech: The Nature of the Quran
Author distinguishes verbal and internal speech and addresses the ruling on denying the Quran.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between verbal speech (الكلام اللفظي) and internal speech (الكلام النفسي) as applied to the Quran, and why it is problematic to simply say 'the Quran is created.'

Core Idea
The Quran has two aspects: the eternal attribute of speech and its temporal verbal expression.

This passage tackles a crucial theological distinction that shaped early Islamic debates: the relationship between the recited words of the Quran and the eternal speech of Allah. Understanding this is key to grasping the Ash'ari position on the createdness of the Quran. Let us read the opening section where the author defines terms and sets the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's presentation of the two kinds of speech and the ruling sink in.
The passage begins by stating that the term 'kalam' is applied to both internal and verbal speech.
2
Identify the two kinds of speech
Find where the author discusses الكلام اللفظي (verbal speech) and الكلام النفسي (internal speech). Notice that the verbal speech is described as originated (حادث) while the internal speech is an eternal attribute.
This distinction is fundamental to the Ash'ari position.
3
Understand the ruling on denial
Notice the author's statement that denying the Quran (the mus-haf) as Allah's speech is kufr unless one means to deny the eternal attribute. This shows sensitivity to different meanings.
4
Learn from the historical example of Imam Ahmad
The passage mentions Imam Ahmad's persecution for refusing to say 'the Quran is created.' Reflect on why such a seemingly simple statement was dangerous without qualification.
This illustrates the practical consequences of theological confusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإلهيات هو: وإطلاقه عليهما؛ قيل: بالاشتراك، وقيل: حقيقي في النفسي مجاز في اللفظي. وعلى كلِّ من أنكر أن ما بين دفتي المصحف كلام الله، فقد كفر، إلا أن يريد أنه ليس الصفة القائمة بذاته تعالى. ومع كون اللفظ الذي نقرؤه حادثاً لا يجوز أن يقال: القرآن حادث إلا في مقام التعليم؛ لأنه يطلق على الصفة القائمة بذاته أيضاً لكن مجازاً على الأرجح، فربما يتوهم من إطلاق أن القرآن حادث أن الصفةَ القائمة بذاته تعالى حادثةٌ، ولذلك ضُرب الإمام أحمد بن حنبل، وحُبس على أن يقول بخلق القرآن فلم يرضَ. وقال السنوسي وغيره من المتقدمين: إن الألفاظ التي نقرؤها تدل على الكلام القديم، وهذا خلافُ التحقيق؛ لأنَّ بعض مدلوله قديم، كما في قوله تعالى: (اللَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الْحَيُّ الْقَيُّومُ) [البقرة: ٢٥٥]، وبعض مدلوله حادث كما في قوله تعالى: (إِنَّ قَارُونَ كَانَ مِن قَوْمِ مُوسَى) [القصص: ٧٦]، والتحقيق أن هذه الألفاظ تدلُّ على بعض مدلول الكلام القديم؛ لأنه يدلُّ على جميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، فالألفاظ التي نقرؤها تدلُّ على بعض هذا المدلول، فلو كُشف عنا الحجاب وفهمنا من الكلام القديم طلب إقامة الصلاة مثلاً، نفهم ذلك من قوله تعالى: (أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ) [الأنعام: ٧٢]، ويصح أن يكون المراد أن الكلام اللفظي يدلُّ على الكلام النفسي دلالة عقلية التزامية بحسب العُرف، فإن من أضيف له كلام لفظي، دل عرفاً على أن له كلاماً نفسياً، وقد أُضيف له تعالى كلام لفظي كالقرآن؛ فإنه كلام الله قطعاً؛ بمعنى أنه خلقه في اللوح المحفوظ، فدلَّ التزاماً بحسب العُرف على أن له تعالى كلاماً نفسياً، وهذا هو المراد بقولهم: (القرآن حادث ومدلوله قديمٌ)، فأرادوا بمدلوله الكلام النفسي، وتكفي الإضافة الإجمالية وإن لم يكن اللفظي قائماً بالذات. وفهم القرافي أن المراد المدلول الوضعي فقال: (منه قديم وهو ذات الله وصفاته، وحادث كخلق السماوات ومستحيل كقوله تعالى: (اتَّخَذَ الرَّحْمَنُ وَلَدًا) [مريم: ٨٨]، كما بسطه العلامة الملوي. (١) قال الإمام السنوسي في شرح الكبرى (ص) (٢٧١) والذي استقر عليه رأي الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري أنه مشترك، واختار المعتزلة أنه حقيقة في اللفظ، بدليل تبادره عند الإطلاق إلى الفهم، ولا يمتنع أن يكون حقيقة لغوية في النفسي، وحقيقة عرفية في اللفظي (٢) في (أ) و(ج): (استلزامية) بدل (التزامية). والحاصل أن للألفاظ التي نقرؤها دلالتين: أولاهما: التزامية عقلية عرفاً؛ كدلالة اللفظ على حياة اللافظ، والمدلول بهذه الدلالة هو الكلام القديم، وهذا محملُ كلام السنوسي ومن تبعه. وثانيهما: وضعيةٌ لفظية، والمدلول بهذه الدلالة بعضه قديم وبعضه حادث، وهذا محمل كلام القرافي وغيره. فلا تنافي بين القولين كما يصرح به بعض حواشي الكبرى والله أعلم.
The divine matters. And its application to both of them: it is said: by homonymy, and it is said: real in the internal (speech) and metaphorical in the verbal. And upon everyone who denies that what is between the two covers of the mus-haf is the speech of Allah, he has disbelieved, unless he means that it is not the attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He. And despite the fact that the utterance which we recite is originated, it is not permissible to say: the Qur'an is originated except in a teaching context; because it is applied to the attribute subsisting in His essence also, but metaphorically on the most preponderant view, and perhaps one might misunderstand from the statement that the Qur'an is originated that the attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, is originated. Therefore Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal was beaten and imprisoned for saying that the Qur'an is created, but he did not consent. And al-Sanusi and others among the early scholars said: the words which we recite indicate the eternal speech. This is contrary to verification; because some of its referent is eternal, as in His statement, exalted is He: (Allah, there is no god but He, the Living, the Self-Subsisting) [al-Baqara: 255], and some of its referent is originated, as in His statement, exalted is He: (Indeed Qarun was from the people of Moses) [al-Qasas: 76]. And the verification is that these words indicate some of the referent of the eternal speech; because it indicates all the necessary, possible, and impossible things, so the words which we recite indicate some of this referent. If the veil were lifted from us and we understood from the eternal speech the command to establish prayer, for example, we would understand that from His statement, exalted is He: (Establish prayer) [al-An'am: 72]. And it is valid that the intended meaning is that verbal speech indicates internal speech by rational implicative indication according to custom. For if verbal speech is attributed to someone, it indicates customarily that he has internal speech. And verbal speech like the Qur'an has been attributed to Him, exalted is He; for it is the speech of Allah absolutely, meaning that He created it in the Preserved Tablet. So it indicated, by implication according to custom, that He, exalted is He, has internal speech. And this is what is meant by their statement: (The Qur'an is originated and its referent is eternal); they meant by its referent the internal speech. And the general attribution suffices even if the verbal is not subsisting in the essence. And al-Qarafi understood that the intended meaning is the explicit referent, so he said: (Some of it is eternal, which is the essence of Allah and His attributes, and some is originated like the creation of the heavens, and some impossible like His statement, exalted is He: (The Most Merciful has taken a son) [Maryam: 88]), as elaborated by the scholar al-Malawi. (1) Imam al-Sanusi said in Sharh al-Kubra (p. 271): The settled view of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is that it is homonymous, and the Mu'tazila chose that it is real in the verbal, evidenced by its immediate understanding upon unqualified mention, and it is not impossible that it is a linguistic reality in the internal and a customary reality in the verbal. (2) In (A) and (J): (istilzamiyya) instead of (iltizamiyya). The conclusion is that the words which we recite have two indications: The first: a rational implicative one according to custom, like the indication of the utterance of the life of the speaker, and the referent by this indication is the eternal speech. This is the interpretation of the words of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. The second: a verbal explicit one, and the referent by this indication is partly eternal and partly originated. This is the interpretation of the words of al-Qarafi and others. So there is no contradiction between the two statements, as is clarified in some glosses of al-Kubra, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion about the nature of the Quran as the speech of Allah, specifically addressing the relationship between the recited words (الكلام اللفظي) and the eternal attribute of speech (الكلام النفسي). It begins by stating that the term 'kalam' is applied to both the internal attribute and the verbal expression, with different opinions on whether this is homonymous or metaphorical. The author then asserts that denying the Quran to be the speech of Allah constitutes disbelief (kufr), unless one means to deny the eternal attribute. Despite the recited words being created (hadith), it is not permissible to simply say 'the Quran is created' without qualification, because the term 'Quran' is also used for the eternal attribute (though metaphorically). The historical persecution of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal is cited as a consequence of this confusion.

The author critiques al-Sanusi's earlier view that the recited words indicate the eternal speech, showing that some referents (like references to created beings) are themselves created, so the indication cannot be directly of the eternal speech in its entirety. Instead, the correct view is that the recited words indicate some of the referent of the eternal speech, which encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things.

The passage then elaborates on two types of indication (dalalat) of the recited words: 1) An implicative indication (dalala iltizamiyya) according to custom: just as spoken words indicate the life of the speaker, the Quran, being attributed to Allah, indicates by custom that He has an eternal internal speech (الكلام النفسي). This is the understanding of al-Sanusi and his followers. 2) An explicit indication (dalala wad'iyya) by linguistic convention, where the referent of the words is partly eternal (e.g., verses about Allah's essence) and partly created (e.g., verses about created beings) and partly impossible (e.g., verses attributing a son to Allah). This is the understanding of al-Qarafi and others.

The author concludes that there is no contradiction between these two views when properly understood, as they pertain to different aspects of the Quran's indication. Footnotes provide additional context on al-Ash'ari's position and textual variants.

Key Terms
الكلام اللفظي
الكلام النفسي
حادث
قديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that denying the Quran as Allah's speech is always disbelief, regardless of what is meant.
Correction: The passage clarifies that disbelief applies only if one denies that the mus-haf is the speech of Allah in the sense of being His attribute; if one denies it only as the eternal attribute, that is a different matter.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal beaten and imprisoned?
A) He denied that the Quran is the speech of Allah.
B) He said the Quran is created without qualification.
C) He refused to say the Quran is created.
D) He claimed the Quran was created in the Preserved Tablet.

Key Takeaway
The Quran is both the eternal attribute of speech (internal) and the created verbal expression (external); one must be careful not to deny the former or to call the latter 'created' without qualification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you realize that the very words you utter are created, yet they point to the eternal speech of Allah? How does this awareness change the reverence you feel in your recitation?
?
The passage warns against carelessly saying 'the Quran is created' because it could lead to misunderstanding. In your own theological conversations, are you careful to consider how your words might be interpreted, or do you speak without thinking about the deeper implications?
?
Imam Ahmad was beaten and imprisoned for refusing to say what he knew was false. If you were pressured to compromise your creed for safety, would you have the same resolve? What is the state of your heart's conviction regarding the speech of Allah?

Recited Words Indicate Part of the Eternal Speech
Author critiques al-Sanusi's view and presents the verification that recited words indicate part of the eternal speech's referent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct position that the recited words of the Quran indicate some of the referent of the eternal speech, not the eternal speech itself in its entirety.

Core Idea
The recited Quran points to a part of the eternal speech's infinite referent.

Al-Sanusi and some early scholars held that the words we recite directly indicate the eternal speech itself. The author of this passage argues that this is not precise, because some verses refer to created events. This lesson explains the correction and shows why the recited words can only point to some of the eternal speech's referent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on al-Sanusi's view and its critique
Read the Arabic and translation where the author states al-Sanusi's claim and then refutes it using examples from the Quran.
Notice the verse examples: some refer to eternal things (Allah's attributes), some to created things (Qarun).
2
Understand the verification (التحقيق)
The author provides the correct understanding: the recited words indicate some of the referent of the eternal speech, because the eternal speech encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Reflect on the 'veil' analogy
The author mentions that if the veil were lifted, we would understand the eternal speech's command directly from the recited words. This highlights the connection between the two.

الإلهيات هو: وإطلاقه عليهما؛ قيل: بالاشتراك، وقيل: حقيقي في النفسي مجاز في اللفظي. وعلى كلِّ من أنكر أن ما بين دفتي المصحف كلام الله، فقد كفر، إلا أن يريد أنه ليس الصفة القائمة بذاته تعالى. ومع كون اللفظ الذي نقرؤه حادثاً لا يجوز أن يقال: القرآن حادث إلا في مقام التعليم؛ لأنه يطلق على الصفة القائمة بذاته أيضاً لكن مجازاً على الأرجح، فربما يتوهم من إطلاق أن القرآن حادث أن الصفةَ القائمة بذاته تعالى حادثةٌ، ولذلك ضُرب الإمام أحمد بن حنبل، وحُبس على أن يقول بخلق القرآن فلم يرضَ. وقال السنوسي وغيره من المتقدمين: إن الألفاظ التي نقرؤها تدل على الكلام القديم، وهذا خلافُ التحقيق؛ لأنَّ بعض مدلوله قديم، كما في قوله تعالى: (اللَّهُ لَا إِلَهَ إِلَّا هُوَ الْحَيُّ الْقَيُّومُ) [البقرة: ٢٥٥]، وبعض مدلوله حادث كما في قوله تعالى: (إِنَّ قَارُونَ كَانَ مِن قَوْمِ مُوسَى) [القصص: ٧٦]، والتحقيق أن هذه الألفاظ تدلُّ على بعض مدلول الكلام القديم؛ لأنه يدلُّ على جميع الواجبات والجائزات والمستحيلات، فالألفاظ التي نقرؤها تدلُّ على بعض هذا المدلول، فلو كُشف عنا الحجاب وفهمنا من الكلام القديم طلب إقامة الصلاة مثلاً، نفهم ذلك من قوله تعالى: (أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ) [الأنعام: ٧٢]، ويصح أن يكون المراد أن الكلام اللفظي يدلُّ على الكلام النفسي دلالة عقلية التزامية بحسب العُرف، فإن من أضيف له كلام لفظي، دل عرفاً على أن له كلاماً نفسياً، وقد أُضيف له تعالى كلام لفظي كالقرآن؛ فإنه كلام الله قطعاً؛ بمعنى أنه خلقه في اللوح المحفوظ، فدلَّ التزاماً بحسب العُرف على أن له تعالى كلاماً نفسياً، وهذا هو المراد بقولهم: (القرآن حادث ومدلوله قديمٌ)، فأرادوا بمدلوله الكلام النفسي، وتكفي الإضافة الإجمالية وإن لم يكن اللفظي قائماً بالذات. وفهم القرافي أن المراد المدلول الوضعي فقال: (منه قديم وهو ذات الله وصفاته، وحادث كخلق السماوات ومستحيل كقوله تعالى: (اتَّخَذَ الرَّحْمَنُ وَلَدًا) [مريم: ٨٨]، كما بسطه العلامة الملوي. (١) قال الإمام السنوسي في شرح الكبرى (ص) (٢٧١) والذي استقر عليه رأي الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري أنه مشترك، واختار المعتزلة أنه حقيقة في اللفظ، بدليل تبادره عند الإطلاق إلى الفهم، ولا يمتنع أن يكون حقيقة لغوية في النفسي، وحقيقة عرفية في اللفظي (٢) في (أ) و(ج): (استلزامية) بدل (التزامية). والحاصل أن للألفاظ التي نقرؤها دلالتين: أولاهما: التزامية عقلية عرفاً؛ كدلالة اللفظ على حياة اللافظ، والمدلول بهذه الدلالة هو الكلام القديم، وهذا محملُ كلام السنوسي ومن تبعه. وثانيهما: وضعيةٌ لفظية، والمدلول بهذه الدلالة بعضه قديم وبعضه حادث، وهذا محمل كلام القرافي وغيره. فلا تنافي بين القولين كما يصرح به بعض حواشي الكبرى والله أعلم.
The divine matters. And its application to both of them: it is said: by homonymy, and it is said: real in the internal (speech) and metaphorical in the verbal. And upon everyone who denies that what is between the two covers of the mus-haf is the speech of Allah, he has disbelieved, unless he means that it is not the attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He. And despite the fact that the utterance which we recite is originated, it is not permissible to say: the Qur'an is originated except in a teaching context; because it is applied to the attribute subsisting in His essence also, but metaphorically on the most preponderant view, and perhaps one might misunderstand from the statement that the Qur'an is originated that the attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, is originated. Therefore Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal was beaten and imprisoned for saying that the Qur'an is created, but he did not consent. And al-Sanusi and others among the early scholars said: the words which we recite indicate the eternal speech. This is contrary to verification; because some of its referent is eternal, as in His statement, exalted is He: (Allah, there is no god but He, the Living, the Self-Subsisting) [al-Baqara: 255], and some of its referent is originated, as in His statement, exalted is He: (Indeed Qarun was from the people of Moses) [al-Qasas: 76]. And the verification is that these words indicate some of the referent of the eternal speech; because it indicates all the necessary, possible, and impossible things, so the words which we recite indicate some of this referent. If the veil were lifted from us and we understood from the eternal speech the command to establish prayer, for example, we would understand that from His statement, exalted is He: (Establish prayer) [al-An'am: 72]. And it is valid that the intended meaning is that verbal speech indicates internal speech by rational implicative indication according to custom. For if verbal speech is attributed to someone, it indicates customarily that he has internal speech. And verbal speech like the Qur'an has been attributed to Him, exalted is He; for it is the speech of Allah absolutely, meaning that He created it in the Preserved Tablet. So it indicated, by implication according to custom, that He, exalted is He, has internal speech. And this is what is meant by their statement: (The Qur'an is originated and its referent is eternal); they meant by its referent the internal speech. And the general attribution suffices even if the verbal is not subsisting in the essence. And al-Qarafi understood that the intended meaning is the explicit referent, so he said: (Some of it is eternal, which is the essence of Allah and His attributes, and some is originated like the creation of the heavens, and some impossible like His statement, exalted is He: (The Most Merciful has taken a son) [Maryam: 88]), as elaborated by the scholar al-Malawi. (1) Imam al-Sanusi said in Sharh al-Kubra (p. 271): The settled view of Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is that it is homonymous, and the Mu'tazila chose that it is real in the verbal, evidenced by its immediate understanding upon unqualified mention, and it is not impossible that it is a linguistic reality in the internal and a customary reality in the verbal. (2) In (A) and (J): (istilzamiyya) instead of (iltizamiyya). The conclusion is that the words which we recite have two indications: The first: a rational implicative one according to custom, like the indication of the utterance of the life of the speaker, and the referent by this indication is the eternal speech. This is the interpretation of the words of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. The second: a verbal explicit one, and the referent by this indication is partly eternal and partly originated. This is the interpretation of the words of al-Qarafi and others. So there is no contradiction between the two statements, as is clarified in some glosses of al-Kubra, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 180

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal discussion about the nature of the Quran as the speech of Allah, specifically addressing the relationship between the recited words (الكلام اللفظي) and the eternal attribute of speech (الكلام النفسي). It begins by stating that the term 'kalam' is applied to both the internal attribute and the verbal expression, with different opinions on whether this is homonymous or metaphorical. The author then asserts that denying the Quran to be the speech of Allah constitutes disbelief (kufr), unless one means to deny the eternal attribute. Despite the recited words being created (hadith), it is not permissible to simply say 'the Quran is created' without qualification, because the term 'Quran' is also used for the eternal attribute (though metaphorically). The historical persecution of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal is cited as a consequence of this confusion.

The author critiques al-Sanusi's earlier view that the recited words indicate the eternal speech, showing that some referents (like references to created beings) are themselves created, so the indication cannot be directly of the eternal speech in its entirety. Instead, the correct view is that the recited words indicate some of the referent of the eternal speech, which encompasses all necessary, possible, and impossible things.

The passage then elaborates on two types of indication (dalalat) of the recited words: 1) An implicative indication (dalala iltizamiyya) according to custom: just as spoken words indicate the life of the speaker, the Quran, being attributed to Allah, indicates by custom that He has an eternal internal speech (الكلام النفسي). This is the understanding of al-Sanusi and his followers. 2) An explicit indication (dalala wad'iyya) by linguistic convention, where the referent of the words is partly eternal (e.g., verses about Allah's essence) and partly created (e.g., verses about created beings) and partly impossible (e.g., verses attributing a son to Allah). This is the understanding of al-Qarafi and others.

The author concludes that there is no contradiction between these two views when properly understood, as they pertain to different aspects of the Quran's indication. Footnotes provide additional context on al-Ash'ari's position and textual variants.

Key Terms
قديم
حادث
مدلول
الكلام القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may believe that the recited Quran directly and wholly indicates the eternal speech of Allah.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the recited words indicate only part of the referent of the eternal speech, because the eternal speech covers all things, while the recited words cover specific subjects, some created.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject al-Sanusi's view that the recited words indicate the eternal speech?
A) Because the eternal speech is uncreated while the recited words are created.
B) Because some of the referent of the recited words is created, not eternal.
C) Because the recited words are metaphorical, not real.
D) Because al-Sanusi was not an authority.

Key Takeaway
The recited Quran indicates a portion of the eternal speech's referent, not the eternal speech itself in its entirety, resolving the issue of verses that mention created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite verses like the story of Qarun, do you feel you are connecting to an eternal attribute or to a temporal event? How does this lesson's distinction challenge you to reconsider the weight of every verse you recite?
?
If you truly believed that behind every recited verse lies an infinite referent from the eternal speech, how would your attentiveness in recitation change? What is holding you back from that depth of focus?
?
This lesson says that if the veil were lifted, you would understand the eternal command directly from the recited words. If you do not need a veil to hear the command 'Establish prayer' now, what is preventing you from obeying it as if it were spoken to you directly at this moment?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
6 min
The Two Indications of the Recited Quran
Author enumerates two indications of recited Quran and reconciles scholarly views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of indication (implicative and explicit) of the recited Quran and how they reconcile the positions of al-Sanusi and al-Qarafi.

Core Idea
The recited Quran has two indications: implicative (pointing to the eternal speech) and explicit (pointing to varied realities).

After discussing the nature of the Quran's speech and the correct understanding of its referent, the author now synthesizes the discussion into two distinct ways the recited words convey meaning. This final part resolves the apparent disagreement between scholars like al-Sanusi and al-Qarafi by showing that each was focusing on a different aspect of indication.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'والحاصل' to the end. Notice how the author enumerates the two indications.
This is a clear enumeration, so pay attention to the structure.
2
Identify the first indication: implicative (التزامية)
The first indication is a rational implicative one according to custom. The example is that verbal speech implies the life of the speaker. Similarly, the Quran implies that Allah has internal speech (الكلام النفسي).
This is the view of al-Sanusi.
3
Identify the second indication: explicit (وضعية)
The second indication is by linguistic convention (وضع), where the words directly refer to meanings. Some of these meanings are eternal (Allah's essence and attributes), some are created (events), and some impossible (claims of falsehood).
This is the view of al-Qarafi.
4
Understand the reconciliation
The author states that there is no contradiction between the two statements because they refer to different aspects of the Quran's indication. One points to the eternal attribute behind the words, the other to the literal content.
This shows the depth of scholarship in reconciling apparent differences.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the second indication (وضعية) of the recited Quran point to?
A) The eternal speech of Allah.
B) The life of the speaker.
C) Partly eternal, partly originated, and partly impossible referents.
D) Only necessary truths.

?

How will you use this distinction to resolve that tension starting today?

The Divine Attribute of Hearing: Definition and Scope
Author defines hearing and discusses its object with two scholarly views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Allah's attribute of hearing and its object according to different theological views.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing encompasses all existents, not only sounds.

In this lesson, we explore the divine attribute of hearing (al-sam') as presented by the author. The passage defines hearing as an eternal attribute that relates to all existents, including sounds and essences. We will also examine a differing interpretation from al-Sa'd and how it can be reconciled.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition
Read the Arabic passage defining hearing as an eternal attribute conjoined to speech. Understand that it subsists in Allah's essence and relates to existents.
Notice the phrase 'تتعلق بالموجودات' which indicates the broad scope.
2
Identify the object according to al-Sanusi
The passage states that hearing relates to 'الموجودات: الأصوات وغيرها كالذوات'. That is, all existents including sounds and essences.
3
Analyze al-Sa'd's statement
Al-Sa'd says hearing relates to 'المسموعات'. Two interpretations are offered: either sounds only (in our regard) or all existents (in Allah's regard). Note which agrees with al-Sanusi.
The author leans toward the interpretation that aligns with al-Sanusi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٦. صفة السمع ودليلها] قوله: (السَّمْعُ) معطوف على الكلام بحذف حرف العطف؛ أي: وكذا السمع، فهو مثل ما ذكر في وجوب اتصافه تعالى به وهو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالموجودات: الأصوات وغيرها كالذوات، كما سيأتي في قوله: (وكل موجود أنط للسمع به) (١)، وهذه طريقة السنوسي ومن تبعه. وقال السعد: تتعلق بالمسموعات (٣)، فيحتمل أن مراده بالمسموعات في حقنا وهي الأصوات، فيكون مخالفاً لطريقة السنوسي ومن تبعه، ويحتمل أن مراده المسموعات في حقه تعالى وهي الموجودات الأصوات وغيرها، فيكون موافقاً لطريقة السنوسي، فيسمعُ سبحانه وتعالى من الأصوات والذوات؛ بمعنى أن كلا منهما منكشف له تعالى بسمعه. ويجب أن نعتقد أن الانكشاف بالسمع غير الانكشاف بالبصر، وأن كلا منهما غيرُ الانكشاف بالعلم، ولكلّ حقيقةً يُفوض علمها الله تعالى وليس الأمر على ما نعهده.
[6. The attribute of hearing and its proof] His statement: (السَّمْعُ) is conjoined to 'speech' by omitting the conjunction; i.e., 'and likewise hearing'. So it is like what was mentioned regarding the obligation of His description, exalted is He, with it, and it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, that relates to existents: sounds and other things like essences, as will come in his statement: (And every existent is encompassed by hearing) (1). This is the way of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. And al-Sa'd said: it relates to the heard things (masmu'at) (3). It is possible that by 'heard things' he means in our regard, which are sounds, and thus he differs from the way of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. And it is possible that by 'heard things' he means in His regard, exalted is He, which are existents: sounds and other things, and thus he agrees with the way of al-Sanusi. So He, glorified and exalted is He, hears sounds and essences, meaning that both are disclosed to Him, exalted is He, by His hearing. And we must believe that disclosure by hearing is different from disclosure by sight, and that each of them is different from disclosure by knowledge, and each has a reality whose knowledge is delegated to Allah, exalted is He, and it is not as we are accustomed.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage begins a new section on the divine attribute of hearing (al-sam'). It first connects the term 'hearing' to the previous discussion on speech, noting that it is conjoined in the same manner as speech was treated. The author defines hearing as an eternal attribute subsisting in Allah's essence that relates to all existents (al-mawjudat), including sounds and other things such as essences (dhawat). This is attributed to the way of al-Sanusi and his followers, who hold that hearing encompasses everything that exists, not limited to sounds.

The author then presents the view of al-Sa'd (probably al-Sa'd al-Taftazani), who states that hearing relates to 'masmu'at' (things that are heard). Two interpretations are offered: either al-Sa'd means 'heard things' in our human experience, i.e., sounds, which would contradict al-Sanusi's view; or he means 'heard things' in relation to Allah, i.e., all existents (sounds and non-sounds), which would agree with al-Sanusi. The author leans toward the latter interpretation, concluding that Allah hears both sounds and essences, meaning that all things are disclosed (munakashif) to Him through His hearing.

Finally, the passage insists on a distinction among three modes of disclosure: by hearing, by sight, and by knowledge. Each is a distinct reality, the exact nature of which is delegated to Allah (tafwid) and not to be analogized to human perception. This guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih) and emphasizes the transcendence of Allah's attributes.

Key Terms
السمع
المسموعات
الموجودات
الأصوات
الذوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's hearing is limited to sounds only, like human hearing.
Correction: According to al-Sanusi and the preferred interpretation, Allah's hearing encompasses all existents, including essences, not just sounds.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sanusi's way, what is the object of Allah's attribute of hearing?
A) Only sounds
B) All existents: sounds and essences
C) Only what is audible to humans
D) Only essences

Key Takeaway
Allah's hearing encompasses all existents, not merely sounds, and this is the position of al-Sanusi.

Disclosure by Hearing, Sight, and Knowledge: The Principle of Tafwid
Author distinguishes disclosure by hearing, sight, and knowledge, and mandates tafwid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the three modes of divine disclosure and the necessity of tafwid regarding their reality.

Core Idea
Disclosure by hearing, sight, and knowledge are distinct realities known only to Allah.

After establishing the scope of hearing, the author emphasizes that Allah's disclosure of things through hearing is different from disclosure through sight or knowledge. This lesson unpacks that distinction and the proper attitude of tafwid (delegation) that avoids anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Understand what disclosure means
The passage says Allah hears sounds and essences, meaning they are 'disclosed' (munakashif) to Him by His hearing. Note that disclosure here refers to Allah's knowledge of things through each attribute.
Disclosure is a technical term indicating that the attribute makes the object known to Allah.
2
Distinguish the three disclosures
The passage explicitly states that disclosure by hearing is different from disclosure by sight, and both are different from disclosure by knowledge. Each is a unique reality.
This guards against equating the attributes or reducing them to one.
3
Apply the principle of tafwid
The author concludes that each disclosure has a reality whose knowledge is delegated to Allah, and it is not like what we are accustomed to in human experience. We affirm the attributes without modality.
Tafwid means we do not attempt to understand how they are; we leave that to Allah.

Two Types of Signification in Divine Speech
The author explains two types of signification resolving the apparent conflict between Sanusi and Qarafi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two types of signification (implicative and conventional) resolve the apparent contradiction between al-Sanusi and al-Qarafi regarding the divine speech attribute.

Core Idea
The same words signify differently: one points to eternal speech, the other to temporal.

This passage tackles a classic point of confusion in kalam: how can the Qur'an be both eternal and created? The author shows that the apparent conflict between two great scholars, al-Sanusi and al-Qarafi, disappears once we recognize that they are referring to different types of signification. Let's unpack this concise yet profound explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get its flow. Then read the English translation. Notice the clear structure: 'first' and 'second' categories leading to a conclusion.
The passage is dense but logically ordered.
2
Identify the two types of signification
The passage names two types: 'التزامية' (implicative) and 'وضعية' (conventional). Understand what each signifies and how they relate to the debate.
The first signification points to the eternal speech; the second points to partly eternal and partly originated.
3
See how the conflict is resolved
The author states that al-Sanusi uses the first signification, al-Qarafi the second. Therefore, there is no contradiction between their statements.
4
Reflect on the broader significance
Consider why this distinction matters: it preserves both the eternity of Allah's speech and the temporal nature of the recited Qur'an without contradiction.
This is a classic example of how careful definition resolves theological disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن للألفاظ التي نقرؤها دلالتين : أولاهما : التزامية عقلية عرفاً ؛ كدلالة اللفظ على حياة اللافظ، والمدلول بهذه الدلالة هو الكلام القديم، وهذا محملُ كلام السنوسي ومن تبعه . وثانيهما: وضعيةٌ لفظية، والمدلول بهذه الدلالة بعضه قديم وبعضه حادث، وهذا محمل كلام القرافي وغيره. فلا تنافي بين القولين كما يصرح به بعض حواشي الكبرى والله أعلم.
The result is that the words we read have two significations: the first is a rational implicative signification by convention, like the word's indication of the life of the speaker, and the signified by this signification is the eternal speech, and this is the interpretation of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. The second is a verbal conventional signification, and the signified by this signification is partly eternal and partly originated, and this is the interpretation of al-Qarafi and others. So there is no contradiction between the two statements, as is declared by some glosses on al-Kubra, and God knows best.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage clarifies an apparent conflict in defining the divine speech attribute. The author identifies two distinct types of signification (دلالة) for the words we recite (i.e., the Qur'an). The first is an implicative (التزامية) signification that is rational and customary: the utterance implies the life of the speaker, and the signified is the eternal speech (الكلام القديم). This is the approach of al-Sanusi and his followers. The second is a verbal conventional (وضعية) signification: the signified is partly eternal and partly temporal. This is the approach of al-Qarafi and others. The author resolves the supposed contradiction by showing that each scholar is interpreting the signification differently, so both views are acceptable. He cites glosses on al-Kubra for support.

Key Terms
دلالة التزامية
دلالة وضعية
كلام قديم
كلام حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that al-Sanusi and al-Qarafi directly contradict each other on the nature of the Qur'an.
Correction: They do not contradict because they are referring to different types of signification: Sanusi focuses on implicative signification (leading to eternal speech), while Qarafi focuses on conventional signification (leading to partly temporal).

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the first type of signification (التزامية) indicate?
A) The life of the speaker and the temporal speech
B) The life of the speaker and the eternal speech
C) Only the eternal speech
D) Only the temporal speech

Key Takeaway
The two types of signification show that al-Sanusi and al-Qarafi are not in contradiction because each refers to a different aspect of the divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Qur'an, do you feel the weight of its eternal origin in your heart, or are you merely focused on the temporal words? How can you cultivate both awarenesses as you recite?
?
The passage shows that apparent contradictions in religious scholarship often dissolve when we understand deeper distinctions. Will you seek to understand the underlying distinctions rather than dismiss them?
?

God's Hearing: Definition and Two Views
The author defines the eternal hearing attribute and presents two scholarly opinions on its objects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the divine attribute of Hearing as an eternal attribute, the two scholarly opinions on what it relates to, and how it differs from temporal hearing.

Core Idea
God's hearing is eternal, all-encompassing, and unlike any physical hearing.

In this passage, the author defines the divine attribute of Hearing, clarifying that it is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence. He then presents two views on what exactly this hearing encompasses—whether it relates to all existents or only to sounds. Finally, he contrasts this eternal hearing with temporal hearing in humans, emphasizing that God's hearing is unique and beyond our ordinary experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage begins by linking hearing to speech via a conjunction.
2
Identify the definition of eternal hearing
Find where the author defines hearing as an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence and relating to existents.
The definition includes both sounds and essences.
3
Compare the two scholarly opinions
Note the positions of al-Sanusi and al-Sa‘d (al-Taftazani) on what hearing relates to. Consider how the author reconciles them.
Al-Sa‘d's view may be interpreted in two ways.
4
Understand the distinct disclosures
The passage emphasizes that disclosure by hearing, sight, and knowledge are all distinct. Reflect on why this distinction matters for God's attributes.
The reality of each is delegated to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (السَّمْعُ) معطوف على الكلام بحذف حرف العطف؛ أي: وكذا السمع، فهو مثل ما ذكر في وجوب اتصافه تعالى به وهو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالموجودات: الأصوات وغيرها كالذوات، كما سيأتي في قوله : (وكل موجود أنط للسمع به)، وهذه طريقة السنوسي ومن تبعه، وقال السعد: تتعلق بالمسموعات، فيحتمل أن مراده بالمسموعات في حقنا وهي الأصوات، فيكون مخالفاً لطريقة السنوسي ومن تبعه، ويحتمل أن مراده المسموعات في حقه تعالى وهي الموجودات الأصوات وغيرها، فيكون موافقاً لطريقة السنوسي، فيسمعُ سبحانه وتعالى من الأصوات والذوات؛ بمعنى أن كلا منهما منكشف له بسمعه. ويجب اعتقاد أن الانكشاف بالسمع غير الانكشاف بالبصر، وأن كلا منهما غيرُ الانكشاف بالعلم، ولكلّ حقيقةً يُفوض علمها الله تعالى وليس الأمر على ما نعهده. وما ذكر من التعريف للسمع القديم وأما السمع الحادث فهو : قوةٌ مودعة في العصب المفروش في مقعر الصماخ، تدرك بها الأصوات على وجه العادة، وقد يدرك بها غير الأصوات، فقد سمع سيدنا موسى كلام الله القديم وهو ليس بحرف ولا بصوت.
His statement: (Al-Sam‘, Hearing) is conjoined to al-Kalam (Speech) with the conjunction omitted; i.e., likewise Hearing, so it is like what was mentioned regarding the necessity of His – exalted is He – being described with it, and it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, which relates to existents: sounds and others like essences, as will come in his statement: 'And every existent is audible to Him by hearing.' This is the method of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. Al-Sa‘d said: it relates to audible things (al-masmu‘at). It is possible that by 'audible things' in our right he means sounds, so he would be opposing the method of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. And it is possible that by 'audible things' in His right, exalted is He, he means existents, both sounds and others, so he would be agreeing with the method of al-Sanusi. So He, glorified and exalted, hears sounds and essences; meaning that each of them is disclosed to Him by His hearing. And one must believe that the disclosure by hearing is different from the disclosure by sight, and that each of them is different from disclosure by knowledge, and each has a reality whose knowledge is delegated to God, exalted is He, and it is not as we are accustomed to. And what has been mentioned is the definition of eternal hearing. As for temporal hearing, it is a power lodged in the nerve spread in the concavity of the auditory meatus, by which sounds are perceived by way of habit, and it may perceive other than sounds, for our master Moses heard the eternal speech of God which is neither a letter nor a sound.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of Hearing (السمع). It begins by noting that 'hearing' is conjoined to 'speech' in the text. It is an eternal attribute (صفة أزلية) subsisting in God's essence. It relates to all existents (الموجودات) including sounds and essences (الذوات). The author presents two views: al-Sanusi holds that hearing relates to all existents; al-Sa‘d (al-Taftazani) holds that it relates only to audible things (المسموعات). However, this could mean audible to us (sounds) or audible to God (all existents). The author prefers the latter interpretation, reconciling al-Sa‘d with al-Sanusi. He emphasizes that the disclosure (الانكشاف) through hearing is distinct from that through sight and through knowledge. Then he contrasts the eternal hearing with temporal hearing (السمع الحادث), which is a physical power in the ear that perceives sounds by habit, and can also perceive other things, as exemplified by Moses hearing God's eternal speech without letters or sound.

Key Terms
السمع
صفة أزلية
المسموعات
الانكشاف
سمع حادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think God's hearing is like human hearing, i.e., a physical process involving ears and sound waves.
Correction: The author emphasizes that God's hearing is an eternal attribute, not a temporal power, and that its disclosure is fundamentally different from human hearing. Moreover, it relates to all existents, not just sounds.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the object of the divine hearing attribute according to al-Sanusi?
A) Only sounds
B) Only essences
C) All existents including sounds and essences
D) Only audible things in the physical sense

Key Takeaway
God's hearing is an eternal, all-encompassing attribute that discloses all existents to Him, and it is fundamentally different from temporal human hearing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Will you now speak with deeper honesty, knowing He hears not just sounds but the reality of your being? Commit to one prayer where you pause and reflect that He hears your innermost state.

How will you adjust your awareness of His presence in your daily actions?

The Divine Attribute of Seeing: Definition, Two Views, and Contrast with Human Sight
Definition of eternal sight, two views, and contrast with human sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the eternal attribute of sight, the two scholarly views on what it encompasses, and how it differs from temporal human sight.

Core Idea
God's eternal sight sees all existents, even the most hidden, unlike human sight which is a created physical power.

This passage defines the divine attribute of seeing (al-basar) and presents two interpretations regarding its objects. The author affirms al-Sanusi's view that it relates to all existents, and then contrasts it with the created human sight. This lesson establishes the transcendence of divine perception.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the use of 'thumma' as a conjunction, not signifying order.
The author explains that 'thumma' simply means 'and' in this context.
2
Identify the two views on the objects of divine sight
Find al-Sanusi's view (sight relates to all existents) and al-Sa'd's view (sight relates to visible things). Understand the reconciliation offered by the author.
Al-Sa'd's view may be understood in two ways – if he means visible to us, he differs; if he means visible to God, he agrees.
3
Distinguish eternal sight from temporal sight
Notice that eternal sight is an attribute of God, while temporal sight is a physical power in the eyes that perceives lights, colors, etc. by God's creation.
The author emphasizes that God sees all things, even sounds and the most hidden movements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ البَصَرْ) معطوف على الكلام، و(ثم) بمعنى الواو؛ لأنَّ صفاته تعالى لا ترتيب فيها، فالمعنى: وكذا البصر فهو مثل ما ذكر في وجوب اتصافه تعالى به، وهو صفة أزلية قائمة بذاته تعالى تتعلق بالموجودات الذوات وغيرها، كما يُعلم من قوله فيما يأتي : كذا البصر كما هو طريقة السنوسي ومن تبعه . وقال السعد: تتعلق بالمبصرات فيحتمل أن مراده المبصرات في حقنا؛ وهي الذوات والألوان فيكون مخالفاً لطريقة السنوسي ومن تبعه، ويحتمل أن مراده المبصرات في حقه تعالى؛ وهي الموجودات الذوات وغيرها، فيكون موافقاً لطريقة السنوسي، فيبصر سبحانه وتعالى جميع الموجودات حتى الأصوات، ولو خفية جدا كدبيب النملة السوداء في الليل المظلم، بمعنى أن ذلك منكشفُ الله ببصره. وما ذكر من التعريف للبصر القديم وأما البصر الحادث فهو قوة مخلوقة في العصبتين المجوفتين المتلاقيتين تلاقياً صليبيا هكذا (+) أو المتلاقيتين تلاقي دالين ظهر أحدهما في ظهر الأخرى هكذا ()( ) تدرك بها الأضواء والألوان والأشكال وغير ذلك مما يخلق الله إدراكه في النفس.
His statement: (Then al-Basar, Sight) is conjoined to al-Kalam (Speech), with 'thumma' meaning 'waw', because there is no order in His attributes, exalted is He. So the meaning is: likewise Sight, so it is like what was mentioned regarding the necessity of His being described with it, and it is an eternal attribute subsisting in His essence, exalted is He, which relates to existents: essences and others, as is known from his statement that will come: 'Likewise Sight,' as is the method of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. Al-Sa‘d said: it relates to visible things (al-mubsarat). It is possible that by 'visible things' in our right he means essences and colors, so he would be opposing the method of al-Sanusi and those who follow him. And it is possible that by 'visible things' in His right, exalted is He, he means existents: essences and others, so he would be agreeing with the method of al-Sanusi. So He, glorified and exalted, sees all existents, even sounds, even if very hidden like the crawling of a black ant in the dark night, meaning that this is disclosed to God by His sight. And what has been mentioned is the definition of eternal sight. As for temporal sight, it is a power created in the two hollow nerves that meet in a cross-like meeting, or that meet in the meeting of two dal letters such that the back of one is against the back of the other, by which are perceived lights, colors, shapes, and other things for which God creates perception in the soul.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage defines the divine attribute of Seeing (البصر). It begins by noting that 'thumma' here is used simply as a conjunction, not indicating sequence. Seeing is an eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, relating to all existents, including essences and others. The author again presents al-Sanusi's view that seeing relates to all existents, and al-Sa‘d's view that it relates to visible things (المبصرات). The same reconciliation is offered: if al-Sa‘d means visible to us (essences and colors), he differs; if he means visible to God (all existents), he agrees. The author emphasizes that God sees everything, even the most hidden, like the crawling of a black ant in the dark night. Then he contrasts eternal sight with temporal sight (البصر الحادث), which is a physical power in the eyes (two hollow nerves crossing) that perceives lights, colors, shapes, etc., by divine creation.

Correction: The author states that God's sight is eternal, encompasses all existents including sounds and hidden things, and is not a created physical power.

Divine Hearing and Created Hearing Distinguished
Summarizes divine sight and defines eternal and temporal hearing

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between divine hearing (eternal) and created hearing, and the perfection of all divine attributes.

Core Idea
Allah's hearing is eternal and perfect, unlike human hearing.

This passage builds on the previous discussion of divine sight to affirm the perfection of all divine attributes. It then defines two types of hearing: eternal hearing as an attribute of Allah, and temporal hearing as a physical faculty in creatures. The example of Prophet Moses hearing Allah's speech clarifies that divine speech is not composed of letters or sounds.

Eternal hearing is an attribute of Allah, not a physical sense.
3

Read the definition of temporal hearing as a physical power. Then consider the example of Moses hearing Allah's eternal speech—this shows that divine speech is not made of letters or sounds.
Temporal hearing perceives sounds; divine hearing perceives without physical means.

The Definition of Divine Sight and Its Distinction from Created Sight
Defines eternal and temporal sight with anatomical detail

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine sight is an eternal attribute distinct from human physical sight, and you will be able to define both.

Core Idea
Divine sight sees all things without eyes; human sight depends on physical nerves.

In this passage, the author clarifies the nature of divine sight as an eternal attribute of Allah, contrasting it with the physical, created sight of humans. He also addresses a subtle disagreement among scholars regarding the scope of what divine sight perceives. This distinction is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphic concepts of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning. Then read the translation to ensure understanding.
Note the author's grammatical note on 'ثم'.
2
Identify the two definitions
The passage defines eternal sight (البصر القديم) and temporal sight (البصر الحادث). Locate each definition.
Eternal sight is an attribute of Allah; temporal sight is a physical faculty.
3
Understand the contrast and disagreement
Notice that divine sight sees all existents, even sounds. There is a difference of opinion with Sa'd, who limited it.
The author follows al-Sanusi's broader view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'Then sight' is coordinated with speech, and 'then' is in the meaning of 'and' because His attributes have no order. So the meaning: likewise sight, it is like what was mentioned regarding the necessity of His being described with it, and it is an eternal attribute subsisting by His essence, relating to existent beings and others, as known from his statement later: 'Likewise sight' as is the method of Sanusi and those who followed him... And what has been mentioned of the definition of eternal sight; as for temporal sight, it is a created power in the two hollow nerves that intersect in a crosswise manner like this (+) or intersect like two dal letters with the back of one against the back of the other like this () () by which lights, colors, shapes, and other things that Allah creates in the soul are perceived.

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the attribute of divine sight. It begins with a linguistic note on the word 'ثم' meaning 'and' to avoid implying order. Then it states that sight is an eternal attribute relating to all existents. It mentions a disagreement with Sa'd regarding the scope. Then it gives the definition of eternal sight and contrasts it with created sight, which is a physical power in the optic nerves. The detailed description of the nerve crossing shows the author's engagement with contemporary anatomy.

The Revealed Proof for Speech, Hearing, and Sight
Proves attributes from Quran and refutes Mu'tazila

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran and Hadith prove that Allah has the attributes of speech, hearing, and sight, and how the Mu'tazilite interpretation of divine speech is refuted.

Core Idea
Revelation establishes Allah's attributes through both explicit verses and prophetic traditions.

This passage opens by clarifying that the poet's phrase 'by which hearing came to us' refers to the auditory evidence from revelation for the three attributes: speech, hearing, and sight. It then delves into the verse about Allah speaking to Moses, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that speech is created, and narrates traditions illustrating Moses' profound experience. This lesson focuses on the revealed proofs and their correct interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the opening phrase
Read the opening line where the poet says 'by which hearing came to us'. The author explains that 'hearing' here means the auditory evidence (the revealed proof), not the attribute itself.
Note the distinction between the attribute and the evidence.
2
Analyze the verse about Moses
Examine the verse 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' and the author's commentary: it means Allah removed the veil and made Moses hear the eternal speech, then restored the veil.
This contradicts the Mu'tazilite claim that Allah creates speech in a tree.
3
Refute the Mu'tazila
Understand why the Mu'tazilite interpretation fails: the default in unqualified attribution is reality (ḥaqīqa), so 'Allah spoke' means He actually spoke, not that He created speech in something else.
The author also mentions a narration from al-Quda'i about 140,000 words, clarifying it refers to meanings, not division of speech.
4
Reflect on Moses' experience
Read the traditions about Moses covering his ears and his face shining. These emphasize the overwhelming nature of hearing Allah's speech and support the reality of the attribute.
The author warns that many popular stories about the intimate conversation are false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (بِذِي أَتَانَا السَّمْعُ) أي : بهذه الصفات الثلاثة التي هي الكلام والسمع والبصر، أتانا المسموع؛ أي: الدليل السمعي، فالسمع بمعنى المسموع؛ وهو الدليل السمعي، وليس المراد أن السمع ورد بنفس الصفات؛ لأنه خلافُ الواقع، بل المراد أنه ورد بمشتقاتها، قال تعالى: ﴿وَكَلَّمَ اللهُ مُوسَى تَكْلِيمًا﴾ [النساء: ١٦٤]؛ أي: أزال عنه الحجاب، وأسمعه الكلام القديم ثم أعاد الحجاب وليس المراد أنه تعالى يبتدئ كلاماً ثم يسكت ؛ لأنه لم يزل متكلماً دائماً وأبداً، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم: بأن المعنى أنه تعالى خلق الكلام فى شجرة وأسمعه موسى ويرد كلامهم بأن الأصل في الإطلاق الحقيقة، وما رواه القضاعي، من أن الله ناجى موسى بمئة ألف وأربعين كلمة (١) ، معناه أنه فهم معاني يُعبّر عنها بهذه العدة لا لتبعيض في نفس الكلام، ورُوي أن موسى عليه الصلاة والسلام كان يسدُّ أذنيه عند قدومه من المناجاة لئلا يسمع كلام الخلق لكونه لا يستطيع سماعه؛ لأنه صار عنده كأشد ما يكون من أصوات البهائم المنكرة، بسبب ما ذاق من اللذة التي لا يُحاط بها عند سماع كلام من ليس كمثله شيء، وقد أشرق وجهه من النور، فما رآه أحدٌ إلا عمي، فتبرقع، وبقي البرقع على وجهه إلى أن مات وأكثر ما اشتهر في المناجاة كذب لا يليق بسيدنا موسى، وقال تعالى: ﴿وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ﴾ [الشورى: ١١]، وقد ورد في الحديث: «ارْبَعُوا على أنفسكم؛ فإنكم لا تدعون أصمَّ»، وفي رواية : ولا غائباً، إنما تدعون سميعاً بصيراً (٢) ، ومعنى قوله : اربعوا على أنفسكم»؛ أشفقوا على أنفسكم؛ فهو من معنى قوله تعالى: ﴿ادْعُوا رَبَّكُمْ تَضَرُّعًا وَخُفْيَةً﴾ [الأعراف : ٥٥]، وقد أجمع أهل الملل والأديان على أنه تعالى متكلم وسميع وبصير .، فإن قيل : المدعى أن له تعالى صفتين من صفات المعاني، وهما السمع والبصر، وما في الآية والحديث وانعقد عليه الإجماع أنه تعالى سميع بصير، وهو غير المدعى، أجيب بأن أهل اللغة لا يفهمون من سميع وبصير إلا ذاتاً ثبت لها السمع والبصر؛ لأنَّ إطلاق المشتق وصفاً لشيء يقتضي ثبوت مأخذ الاشتقاق له، فثبت له المدعى بالآية والحديث والإجماع مع اعتبار ما يفهمه أهل اللغة ولا يخفى أنه لا إيطاء في كلام الناظم، بل فيه الجناسُ التام؛ لأن السمع الأول بمعنى الصفة القديمة، والسمع الثاني
His statement: 'By which the hearing came to us' meaning: by these three attributes which are speech, hearing, and sight, the hearing came to us meaning the auditory evidence. So 'hearing' is in the sense of 'what is heard', i.e., the auditory evidence. The meaning is not that the attribute 'hearing' itself came in revelation, because that is contrary to fact, but rather that its derivatives came. He said: 'And Allah spoke to Moses directly' [4:164], meaning: He removed the veil from him and made him hear the eternal speech, then restored the veil. It does not mean that He begins speech then becomes silent, because He has always been speaking eternally, contrary to the Mu'tazila who say that the meaning is that Allah created speech in a tree and made Moses hear it. Their statement is refuted because the default in unqualified attribution is reality. And what al-Quda'i narrated, that Allah conversed with Moses with one hundred forty thousand words (1), its meaning is that he understood meanings expressed by this number, not that the speech itself is divided. And it is narrated that Moses, peace be upon him, used to cover his ears when returning from the intimate conversation so that he would not hear the speech of creation because he could not bear it; for it had become to him like the most unpleasant sounds of animals, due to the pleasure that cannot be encompassed when hearing the speech of the One like whom there is nothing. And his face shone with light, so no one saw him but became blind, so he veiled himself, and the veil remained on his face until he died. Most of what is famous about the intimate conversation is false and unfitting for our master Moses. And He said: 'And He is the Hearing, the Seeing' [42:11], and it has come in the hadith: 'Be gentle with yourselves, for you are not calling a deaf one,' and in another narration: 'nor an absent one, you are calling a Hearing, Seeing one' (2). The meaning of 'be gentle with yourselves' is have pity on yourselves; it is similar to His statement: 'Call upon your Lord humbly and in secret' [7:55]. And the people of all religions have agreed that He is speaking, hearing, and seeing. Then if it is said: The claim is that He has two attributes of meanings, hearing and sight, but what is in the verse and hadith and consensus is that He is Hearing and Seeing, which is different from what is claimed. The answer is that the people of language do not understand from 'Hearing' and 'Seeing' except a being that has hearing and sight, because the use of a derived term as a description for something necessitates the establishment of the source of derivation for it. So the claim is established by the verse, hadith, and consensus, considering what the people of language understand. And it is not hidden that there is no repetition (īṭā') in the poet's words, but rather there is complete pun (jinās tāmm), because the first 'hearing' means the eternal attribute, and the second 'hearing'...
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a proof from revelation for the divine attributes of speech, hearing, and sight. The author explains that the poet's phrase 'بِذِي أَتَانَا السَّمْعُ' means that the auditory evidence conveys these attributes. He then discusses the verse about Moses, refuting the Mu'tazilite interpretation that God's speech is created. He also narrates traditions about Moses' experience. Then he addresses an objection: the revelation says 'Samī' and Baṣīr' not 'al-sam' and al-basar' as attributes. The answer is that the derived terms imply the attributes by linguistic principle. The passage concludes with a linguistic note about the poet's use of 'sam'' in two senses, creating a pun.

Key Terms
الكلام
السمع
البصر
المعتزلة
الحقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the Quran only attributes speech to Allah indirectly, or that Allah's speech is created.
Correction: The passage shows that the verse about Moses is literal and that Allah has spoken directly, and the Mu'tazilite view is refuted.

Checkpoint
How does the author interpret the phrase 'بِذِي أَتَانَا السَّمْعُ'?
A) The attribute of hearing came through revelation.
B) The auditory evidence (revealed proof) came to us.
C) The poet is referring to a specific hadith.
D) It means the sense of hearing is proven.

Key Takeaway
Revelation provides direct proof that Allah has the attributes of speech, hearing, and sight, and the Mu'tazilite interpretation of created speech is invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that Moses covered his ears to avoid hearing any other speech after experiencing the divine, does your own practice of reading the Quran reflect that sense of awe and otherness? If not, what would it take for you to approach the Quran as the eternal speech of Allah, not just a book?
?
The Mu'tazila reduced Allah's speech to a created sound in a tree. Do you ever reduce the Quran to a mere historical or cultural document, forgetting that every word directly originates from His eternal attribute? What would change in your life if you truly believed that?
?
If you were granted but one moment to hear Allah's speech directly, would your current spiritual state make you recoil like Moses did, or would you be able to bear it? What does that tell you about the state of your heart and your need to purify it to receive His words?
