The Grammatical Analysis of 'Hayy'
Sanusi argues that 'Hayy' is a predicate, not a coordinate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the word 'Hayy' in the poem is not grammatically coordinated with 'existence' and what its correct grammatical function is.

Core Idea
'Hayy' is a predicate, not an additional attribute of existence.

Sanusi clarifies a potential grammatical confusion. Some might think 'Hayy' is simply another attribute alongside existence, but that would imply a false theological consequence. Instead, he shows it is a predicate for an omitted subject, reinforcing that Allah is essentially living.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Sanusi begins by stating the moral attributes are seven, then focuses on the word 'Hayy'.
The line 'مَا يَشَايُرِيدُ' is likely 'ما يشاء يريد'.
2
Identify the grammatical issue
The author argues that 'Hayy' cannot be a coordinate of 'wujud' (existence). Understand why: it would imply that existence is necessary for Allah, which is false.
3
Learn the correct analysis
Sanusi concludes that 'Hayy' is a khabar (predicate) for a mubtada' (subject) that is omitted and connected with 'fa'. The meaning: 'And when life is necessitated for Him, He is Living.'
This grammatical construction avoids the theological error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
د. الصفات المعنوية سبع]
1 - حَيٌّ عَلِيمٌ قادِرٌ مُرِيدُ سَمِعْ بَصِيرٌ مَا يَشَايُرِيدُ
قوله : ( حي) لا يصح أن يكون معطوفا على الوجود بحذف حرف العطف; لأنه ينحل المعنى, وواجب له حي, وهذا فاسد لأن الله تعالى هو الحي, فتعين أن يكون خبرا لمبتدأ محذوف مقرون بالفاء والتقدير : وحيث وجبت له الحياة فهو حي, والذي ذكره المصنف في شرحه : أنه أراد مجرد بيان الأسماء المأخوذة مما سبق لبيان وجوب قيام الصفة بالموصوف ردًّا على بعض فرق الضلال, حيث قالوا بعدم قيام بعضها بالموصوف كالكلام والإرادة ولم يرد بيان الصفات المعنوية, ولذا لم يقل : كونه حيا; لأن عد الصفات المعنوية إنما يتمشى على قول مثبت الأحوال, جمع حال; وهي لا موجودة ولا معدومة بل واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم, وعليه جرى السنوسي في الصغرى, حيث قال : وكونه قادرا .. إلى آخره.
D. The moral attributes are seven: 1. Living, Knowing, Powerful, Willing, Hearing, Seeing, whatever He wills. His saying: (Living) is not correct to be coordinated with 'existence' by omitting the conjunction; because the meaning would dissolve, and 'Living is necessary for Him', and this is invalid because Allah, exalted, is the Living. So it is determined that it is a khabar of a mubtada' that is omitted and connected with fa, and the estimation: And when life is necessitated for Him, He is Living. And what the author mentioned in his commentary: that he intended merely to mention the names derived from what preceded, to explain the necessity of the attribute's subsisting in the described, in refutation of some deviant sects who allowed that some of them do not subsist in the described, such as speech and will, and he did not intend to explain the moral attributes. Therefore he did not say: 'His being living'; because the enumeration of the moral attributes only proceeds according to the opinion of the affirmers of states (ahwal), which are neither existent nor non-existent but an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent. And al-Sanusi followed this in his al-Sughra, where he said: 'And His being powerful... etc.'
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage explains the first line of the poem listing seven moral attributes. The author clarifies that the word 'Hayy' (Living) is not coordinated with 'wujud' (existence) as some might think, because that would imply that existence is necessary for Allah, which is not the case. Instead, it is a predicate for a deleted subject, estimated as 'He is Living when life is necessitated for Him.' The author then explains that the purpose of mentioning these attributes (like Hayy, 'Alim, Qadir, etc.) is not to enumerate the moral attributes (sifat ma'nawiyya) per se, but rather to show that the attribute must subsist in the described, refuting deviant sects that denied subsistence for some attributes like speech and will. The author notes that an actual enumeration of moral attributes would require accepting the theory of 'ahwal' (states), which al-Sanusi adopted in his al-Sughra, where he said 'and His being powerful...' Thus, this passage addresses the correct grammatical analysis of 'Hayy' and the theological purpose behind the list.

Key Terms
مبتدأ محذوف
خبر
معطوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'Hayy' is an additional attribute of existence.
Correction: The passage shows it is a predicate for a deleted subject, not a coordinate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't 'Hayy' be grammatically coordinated with 'existence'?
A) Because it would make the sentence ungrammatical.
B) Because it would imply that existence is necessary for Allah.
C) Because existence is not an attribute.
D) Because it is actually a predicate for a deleted subject.

Key Takeaway
The word 'Hayy' is grammatically a predicate, not an additional coordinate with existence, to avoid the theological error of implying existence is necessary for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is Living,' do you ever subtly imagine His life as a quality added to His existence, like a property of a thing? How does the grammatical precision of this passage challenge you to rethink your mental image of Allah's attributes? Commit to correcting any such thought immediately.
?
This passage shows that a simple grammatical error can lead to a theological distortion. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah where you have accepted comfortable but imprecise language? Take a moment to identify one such area and resolve to study it deeper, not just for knowledge but to safeguard your iman.
?
Sanusi warns that coordinating 'Hayy' with existence dissolves the meaning and leads to an invalid statement. In your daily life, how often do you slip into careless descriptions of Allah without considering their theological implications? Write down one practical habit you will adopt to ensure your words about Allah are precise and reverent.

The Purpose of the Attribute List and the Theory of States
Sanusi explains the purpose of listing attributes and the concept of ahwal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poem lists moral attributes and how that relates to the theory of states (ahwal) and refuting deviant sects.

Core Idea
Listing attributes proves they subsist in Allah, refuting those who deny it.

Sanusi reveals the deeper purpose behind the attribute list. It is not simply enumeration but a theological argument. He also connects to the controversial theory of ahwal, which al-Sanusi used in his smaller work.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage. Notice the contrast between what the author intended and what he did not intend.
2
Understand the refutation of deviant sects
The author's purpose is to show that attributes must subsist in Allah, refuting sects that denied subsistence for some attributes like speech and will.
3
Grasp the theory of ahwal
The author explains that a proper enumeration of moral attributes depends on the theory of states (ahwal), which are neither existent nor non-existent. Al-Sanusi adopted this in his al-Sughra.
This theory is a specific theological position.

According to the passage, what is the main purpose of listing the attributes like 'Hayy', 'Alim', etc.?

?
Cuando lees los nombres de los atributos de Alá, ¿crees realmente que son reales y subsisten en Él, o a veces los consideras meros títulos? ¿Cómo afecta esto a tu adoración y confianza en Él?
?
Las sectas desviadas negaban que algunos atributos como el habla y la voluntad subsistan en Alá. ¿Has dudado inconscientemente de que Alá posea atributos como la misericordia o la justicia de manera real? ¿Qué cambiaría si afirmaras firmemente que son reales?
?
La teoría de los estados (ahwal) es un concepto teológico sutil. ¿Estás dispuesto a profundizar en temas como este para refinar tu comprensión de Alá, o prefieres quedarte en la superficie? ¿Cómo refleja tu enfoque tu amor por conocerlo?

The Threefold Classification on Denying States
Bajuri enumerates the threefold classification on the view negating states.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of things according to the view that denies states (aḥwāl).

Core Idea
When states are denied, everything is either existent, non-existent, or a considerative matter.

Bajuri begins by presenting the classification that results from rejecting the theory of states. This view, which is preponderant in the tradition, reduces the categories of things to just three. Understanding this classification is essential for grasping later discussions on attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening classification
Read the Arabic line establishing the three categories: existents, non-existents, and considerative matters. Notice that the author calls this method 'preponderant'.
The phrase 'بقسميها' refers to the two subtypes of considerative matters, though they are not detailed here.
2
Identify the three categories
List the three categories: existents, non-existents, and considerative matters. Reflect on how this differs from the view that affirms states.
3
Relate to the broader debate
Consider why this classification matters for the discussion of divine attributes. The denial of states simplifies ontology and affects how attributes are understood.
This classification prepares for the explanation of essential attributes (maʿnawiyya) in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعلى القول بنفي الأحوال تكون الأمور ثلاثة: موجودات ومعدومات، وأمور اعتبارية بقسميها، وهذه الطريقة هي الراجحة. ومعنى إنكار المعنوية إنكار زيادتها على المعاني بحيث تكون واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، لا إنكار كونه قادراً مثلاً من أصله؛ لأنَّه مُجمَعٌ عليه فليس فيه خلافٌ إنما الخلاف في زيادته على المعاني . فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على الكون قادراً مثلاً، لكن على القول بثبوت الأحوال تكون واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم لازمة للقدرة، وعلى القول بنفي الأحوال تكون عبارةٌ عن قيام القدرة بالذات فيكون أمراً اعتباريًا، وهذا كله عند أهل السنة وأما عند المعتزلة فهي كناية عن القادرية (۱)؛ أي: كونه قادراً بذاته، وكذا يُقال في الباقي، فهم وإن أنكروا المعاني لم ينكروا القادرية والعالمية وغيرهما، فيقولون: قادر بذاته، وعالم بذاته، إلى غير ذلك؛ ولذلك يقولون: من أنكر المعاني لا يكفر إلا إذا أثبت ضدها، ومن أنكر المعنوية بمعنى القادرية ونحوها كَفَرَ؛ لأنه يلزم من إنكار القادرية إثباتُ الضد، وأما إنكار المعنوية بمعنى الأحوال فهو الحقُّ . وحيث علمت أن المصنف صرّح بأنه أراد مجرد بيان الأسماء، ولم يُرِدْ بيان الصفات المعنوية، علمت أن حمله على بيان المعنوية ليس على ما ينبغي، وإن ذكره الشيخ عبد السلام وغيره، خصوصاً وقد عبر بالحي ... إلى آخره، ولم يعبر يكونه حيا .. إلى آخره، وقد قالوا : صاحب البيت أدرى بالذي فيه . (۱) قوله : ( القادرية) هي تمكنه من الإيجاد والإعدام بذاته. انتهى أجهوري.
And on the view negating states, things are three: existents, non-existents, and considerative matters with their two types, and this method is the preponderant. And the meaning of denying the essential attributes (al-maʿnawiyya) is denying their superaddition to the meanings such that they become an intermediate between the existent and the non-existent, not denying that He is powerful, for example, from its root; because it is agreed upon and there is no dispute in it; the dispute is only in its superaddition to the meanings. The upshot is that they agreed on His being powerful, for example, but on the view affirming states, it is an intermediate between existent and non-existent inherent in power; and on the view negating states, it is an expression of the subsistence of power in the Essence, so it becomes a considerative matter. All this is according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Muʿtazila, it is a metaphor for qādiriyya (1); i.e., His being powerful by His Essence, and similarly for the rest. For they, although they deny the meanings, they do not deny qādiriyya, ʿālimiyya, and others. So they say: powerful by His Essence, knowing by His Essence, etc. Therefore they say: whoever denies the meanings does not become an unbeliever unless he affirms their opposite; and whoever denies al-maʿnawiyya in the sense of qādiriyya and the like becomes an unbeliever, because denial of qādiriyya necessarily entails affirmation of the opposite. As for denying al-maʿnawiyya in the sense of states, it is the truth. And since you know that the author declared that He intended only a bare statement of the names, and did not intend to explain the essential attributes, you know that interpreting it as an explanation of the essential attributes is not appropriate, even if Sheikh ʿAbd al-Salām and others mentioned it, especially since He expressed it with 'the Living' ... etc., and did not express it with 'His being living' ... etc. And they said: the master of the house knows best what is in it. (1) His saying: 'al-qādiriyya' is His ability to bring into existence and annihilate by His Essence. End of al-Ujhūrī.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage presents the classification of things according to those who deny the existence of states (aḥwāl), which is the preponderant view: only three categories—existents, non-existents, and considerative matters (with their two subtypes). It then clarifies that denying 'essential attributes' (al-maʿnawiyya) means denying that they are intermediate entities superadded to the meanings (like power), not denying the attribute itself (e.g., being powerful), which is agreed upon. All schools agree that God is powerful, but they differ on its ontological status: for the affirmers of states, 'being powerful' is a state intermediate between existent and non-existent inherent in power; for the deniers of states, it is a considerative matter expressing the subsistence of power in the Essence; for the Muʿtazila, it is a metaphor for qādiriyya (being powerful by the Essence itself). The Muʿtazila deny the meanings (eternal attributes) but affirm the capability (qādiriyya); thus denying capability leads to disbelief, while denying states is correct. Finally, the author confirms that his work only intended to list names, not to discuss essential attributes, contrary to some interpretations.

Key Terms
موجودات
معدومات
أمور اعتبارية
أحوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many categories are there on the view denying states?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
When states are denied, reality is simplified into three categories: existents, non-existents, and considerative matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that on the view negating states, everything is either existent, non-existent, or considerative. Where in this scheme do you place your own understanding of God's essence? Could it be that some of your assumptions about God are 'considerative matters'—true in the mind but not fully graspable as external existents? Will you spend time today in silence, asking God to help you distinguish between what is really real about Him and what is merely your mental construct?
?
You rely on rational categories to organize your faith. But this lesson reminds you that even the most sophisticated classification—like this threefold division—is a human tool, and the preponderant view is chosen for its simplicity. How does accepting a 'preponderant' theological method affect your personal confidence in your beliefs? Are you willing to submit your intellect to the tradition's wisdom, or do you need every detail to be independently verified? Make a concrete decision this week to trust a received teaching from a qualified scholar without demanding exhaustive proof.

The Meaning of Denying Essential Attributes
Bajuri explains that denying essential attributes means denying their intermediacy, not the attributes themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what it means to deny essential attributes (maʿnawiyya) and how all schools agree on the reality of attributes like power.

Core Idea
Denying essential attributes means denying they are intermediate entities, not denying the attributes themselves.

After establishing the threefold classification, Bajuri clarifies a common misunderstanding. The denial of 'essential attributes' (maʿnawiyya) is often misinterpreted. He explains that it only denies their status as independent intermediaries, not the attributes like power or knowledge themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the clarification
Read from 'ومعنى إنكار المعنوية' to 'فيكون أمرًا اعتباريًا'. Notice the distinction between denying the attribute itself and denying its intermediacy.
2
Identify the common ground
Find the sentence that states the agreement: everyone agrees on God being powerful. The dispute is only about the ontological status of 'being powerful'.
This agreement includes Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila, though they interpret it differently.
3
Compare the three views
Note the three positions: affirming states (states are intermediaries), denying states (considerative matter), and Mu'tazila (metaphor for essential capability).
The Mu'tazila view is previewed here; more detail follows in the next lesson.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Denying essential attributes means denying that God is powerful, knowing, etc.
Correction: It only denies that these predicates are intermediate entities (states) between the attribute and the essence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the common ground among all schools regarding God's attributes?
A) They agree on the existence of states
B) They agree that God is powerful, knowing, etc.
C) They agree that attributes are not real
D) They agree that attributes are accidental

Key Takeaway
All Muslims affirm that God has attributes like power; the debate concerns the ontological status of the predicate 'being powerful'.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You affirm that God is powerful—do you live each day as if that power is real and active, or do your worries and decisions betray a belief that His power is merely an abstract statement?
?
This lesson shows that even the most basic descriptions of God (like 'He is powerful') have deep theological implications. Are you content to repeat these descriptions without striving to understand their reality, or will you dig deeper to know your Lord as He truly is?
?
The debate about the status of predicates might seem academic, but it shapes how you relate to God's attributes. If you treat God's power as just a label, your worship becomes hollow. Will you stop and ask yourself: 'Do I truly believe that God is powerful in a way that demands my complete reliance and awe?'

The Mu'tazila View on Attributes and the Consequence of Denial
Bajuri explains the Mu'tazila view and the ruling on denying qādiriyya, then clarifies the author's intent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazila position on attributes and why denying qādiriyya constitutes disbelief.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila deny eternal meanings but affirm essential capabilities; denying capability leads to kufr.

Bajuri now details the Mu'tazila perspective, which differs from both the affirmers and deniers of states. The Mu'tazila reject the existence of distinct attributes (maʿānī) but affirm that God is powerful by His essence (qādiriyya). The passage also explains the serious consequence of denying this essential capability.

Guided Path
1
Read the Mu'tazila position
Read from 'وهذا كله عند أهل السنة' to 'إلى غير ذلك'. Note how the Mu'tazila interpret 'being powerful' as a metaphor for essential capability.
2
Understand the consequence
Read the statement about who becomes an unbeliever. Distinguish between denying meanings (permissible) and denying qādiriyya (disbelief).
The denial of qādiriyya implies attributing inability to God, which is a contradiction.
3
Read the author's clarification
Read the final part from 'وحيث علمت' to the end. Understand that the author's intent was only to list names, not to discuss essential attributes.
This is a meta-commentary on the proper interpretation of the poem.

According to the passage, what is the consequence of denying qādiriyya (being powerful by essence)?
A) It is a valid theological position

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever, in a moment of trial, thought that God cannot do something or delayed His help? That was a denial of qādiriyya in your heart. Will you repent and affirm that He is powerful by His Essence, without limit, even when your situation seems hopeless?
?
This lesson shows that denying what God has affirmed about Himself—even if not deliberately—leads to disbelief. Is your mental image of God one that fully embraces His attributes as He described, or do you subtly reduce them to fit your reason? Will you commit to studying the Salaf's understanding of attributes and purifying your belief?

Mental Considerations: Derived and Invented
Commentator defines umur i'tibariyya and classifies them into derived and invented.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) and how they relate to external existence.

Core Idea
Mental considerations are either grounded in reality or purely invented by the mind.

Before diving into the theological debate on divine attributes, the commentator establishes a crucial conceptual tool: the distinction between derived and invented mental considerations. This distinction underpins how we understand terms like 'existence' and 'power' when applied to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of mental consideration
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the commentator begins with a definitive statement.
The term 'umur i'tibariyya' is foundational in kalam.
2
Distinguish between the two types
Identify the derived (intiza'i) type, which is abstracted from real external states, and the invented (ikhtira'i) type, which is purely mental.
The example of 'sea of mercury' helps illustrate the invented type.
3
Understand dependence on the considerer
Note that the derived type does not depend on the observer's consideration, while the invented type does.
This has implications for how we view divine attributes.

الإلهيات أمر اعتباري انتزاعي؛ لأنه انتزع من الهيئة الثابتة في الخارج، وأمور اعتبارية اختراعية ك (بحر من زئبق) فهو أمرٌ اعتباري اختراعي؛ لأنه اخترعه الشخص، والقسم الأول لا يتوقف على اعتبار المعتبر وفرض الفارض والقسم الثاني يتوقف على ذلك. وعلى القول بنفي الأحوال تكون الأمور ثلاثة: موجودات ومعدومات، وأمور اعتبارية بقسميها، وهذه الطريقة هي الراجحة. ومعنى إنكار المعنوية إنكار زيادتها على المعاني بحيث تكون واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، لا إنكار كونه قادراً مثلاً من أصله؛ لأنَّه مُجمَعٌ عليه فليس فيه خلافٌ إنما الخلاف في زيادته على المعاني. فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على الكون قادراً مثلاً، لكن على القول بثبوت الأحوال تكون واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم لازمة للقدرة، وعلى القول بنفي الأحوال تكون عبارةٌ عن قيام القدرة بالذات فيكون أمراً اعتباريًا، وهذا كله عند أهل السنة وأما عند المعتزلة فهي كناية عن القادرية (١)؛ أي: كونه قادراً بذاته، وكذا يُقال في الباقي، فهم وإن أنكروا المعاني لم ينكروا القادرية والعالمية وغيرهما، فيقولون: قادر بذاته، وعالم بذاته، إلى غير ذلك؛ ولذلك يقولون: من أنكر المعاني لا يكفر إلا إذا أثبت ضدها، ومن أنكر المعنوية بمعنى القادرية ونحوها كَفَرَ؛ لأنه يلزم من إنكار القادرية إثباتُ الضد، وأما إنكار المعنوية بمعنى الأحوال فهو الحقّ. وحيث علمت أن المصنف صرّح بأنه أراد مجرد بيان الأسماء، ولم يُرِدْ بيان الصفات المعنوية، علمت أن حمله على بيان المعنوية ليس على ما ينبغي، وإن ذكره الشيخ عبد السلام وغيره، خصوصاً وقد عبر بالحي ... إلى آخره، ولم يعبر يكونه حيا .. إلى آخره، وقد قالوا: صاحب البيت أدرى بالذي فيه. للذات، والاعتباري لا يكون قارا للذات كما علمت ويرد على هذا الفرق أن السعد ومن تبعه جعلوا الوجود أمراً اعتباريًا مع كونه قارًا للذات والجواب أن هذه التفرقة عند القائلين بثبوت الأحوال، والظاهر أن السعد ومن تبعه ليسوا من القائلين بثبوت الأحوال، فالأمر الاعتباري عندهم يشمل ما كان قارا للذات، يُؤخذ معظم هذا من حاشية الدسوقي على المصنف، والمراد به (المعظم) الفرق بين الحال والاعتبار فقط. انتهى أجهوري. (١) قوله: (القادرية) هي تمكنه من الإيجاد والإعدام بذاته. انتهى أجهوري.
The divine matters: a mental consideration derived from the state established externally. And there are invented mental considerations like 'a sea of mercury' – it is an invented mental consideration because the person invented it. The first type does not depend on the consideration of the considerer or the supposition of the supposer, while the second type depends on that. According to the negation of states (ahwal), the things are three: existents, non-existents, and mental considerations of both types. This method is the more correct. And the meaning of denying the attributive state (al-ma'nawiyya) is denying its addition to the intelligible meanings (al-ma'ani) such that it would be an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, not denying that He is 'powerful' for example from its root, because it is agreed upon, so there is no disagreement in it; the disagreement is only in its addition to the meanings. So the summary is that they agreed on the existence of being powerful, for example, but according to the affirmation of states, it is an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent necessary for power; and according to the negation of states, it is an expression for the subsistence of power in the essence, so it becomes a mental consideration. All this is according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is a metaphor for power-ability (al-qadiriyya), i.e., His being powerful by His essence. And similarly is said in the rest. So even if they denied the intelligible meanings (al-ma'ani), they did not deny power-ability, knowledge-ability, and others. So they say: 'Powerful by His essence, Knowing by His essence, etc.' Therefore they say: whoever denies the intelligible meanings does not become an unbeliever unless he affirms their opposite; and whoever denies the attributive state in the sense of power-ability and the like becomes an unbeliever, because denying power-ability necessarily entails affirming the opposite. As for denying the attributive state in the sense of states, it is the truth. And since you know that the author explicitly stated that he intended merely the explanation of the names, and did not intend the explanation of the attributive attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya), you know that interpreting him as explaining the attributive state is not as it should be, even if Shaykh Abd al-Salam and others mentioned it, especially since he expressed with 'the Living... etc.' and did not express 'His being living... etc.' And they said: 'The owner of the house knows best what is in it.' For the essence, and the mental consideration is not established for the essence as you know. And this distinction is objected to by the fact that al-Sa'd and those who followed him made existence a mental consideration while it is established for the essence. The answer is that this distinction is according to those who affirm states, and it appears that al-Sa'd and those who followed him are not among those who affirm states, so the mental consideration according to them includes what is established for the essence. Most of this is taken from the gloss of al-Dasuqi on the author. And what is intended by 'most' is the distinction between the state and the consideration only. End of al-Ujhuri. (1) His saying: 'al-qadiriyya is His ability to bring into existence and annihilate by His essence.' End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) followed by a detailed theological debate regarding the nature of divine attributes, specifically the question of states (ahwal) and the position of Ahl al-Sunna versus the Mu'tazila. The commentator begins by defining a mental consideration as either derived (intiza'i) from an external state or invented (ikhtira'i) by the mind, giving the example of 'a sea of mercury'. The first type does not depend on a subject's consideration, while the second does. On the view that rejects states (ahwal), things are divided into three: existents, non-existents, and mental considerations of both types—this is considered the preferred method. The commentator then clarifies that denying 'al-ma'nawiyya' (the attributive state) means denying that it is an additional entity mediating between existent and non-existent, not denying that God is powerful, etc. The disagreement is about the addition of such states to the meanings (ma'ani), not about the reality of the attribute. He then explains the agreement: all agree that God is powerful, etc., but on the view affirming states, it is an intermediary; on the view negating states, it is merely the subsistence of power in the essence (hence a mental consideration). This is the Sunni position. The Mu'tazila treat these as metaphors: 'powerful by His essence' (qadir bi-dhatihi). They deny the additional intelligible meanings (ma'ani) but affirm the power-ability (qadiriyya) and knowledge-ability (alimiyya). They hold that denying the ma'ani does not entail unbelief unless one affirms their opposites, but denying the qadiriyya does entail unbelief because it necessitates affirming the opposite (i.e., inability). Denying ahwal, however, is considered the truth. The commentator then notes that the author (al-Laqqani) only intended to explain the names of God, not the attributive attributes, so interpreting his poem as discussing the attributive state is incorrect, despite what some scholars like Shaykh Abd al-Salam said. He supports this by noting that the author used 'the Living' (al-Hayy) not 'His being living'. The phrase 'the owner of the house knows best' is a proverb. The passage ends with a discussion about the consistency of mental considerations: some objected that al-Sa'd and his followers considered existence a mental consideration even though it is established for the essence. The reply is that this distinction (between mental and real) applies only for proponents of states, and al-Sa'd is not among them—for him, mental considerations include what is established for essence. This is taken from al-Dasuqi's gloss. The footnote clarifies that 'qadiriyya' means ability to create and annihilate by essence.

Key Terms
أمر اعتباري
انتزاعي
اختراعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All mental considerations are purely subjective and arbitrary.
Correction: Some mental considerations, like derived ones, are based on real external states and do not depend on the observer.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which type of mental consideration does not depend on the considerer?
A) Invented
B) Derived
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Derived mental considerations are rooted in external reality and do not require the mind's invention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever catch yourself inventing ideas about God that are not based on scripture or sound reasoning? How can you train yourself to only accept what is derived from revelation?
?
The passage says derived considerations do not depend on the considerer. In your personal beliefs, are you submitting to what is objectively true or constructing your own version of God? What would change if you truly submitted?
?
Have you ever considered that some of your cherished theological opinions might be like 'a sea of mercury'—purely mental inventions with no reality? What would it take for you to abandon them for the derived truth?

Divine Attributes
intermediate
8 min
The Debate on States: Ahl al-Sunna vs Mu'tazila
Commentator explains implications of states denial and compares Sunni and Mu'tazila views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold division of things when states are denied, and the differences between Ahl al-Sunna and Mu'tazila regarding divine attributes.

Core Idea
The key dispute is whether divine attributes require an intermediary state or are direct expressions of God's essence.

The commentator now applies the concept of mental considerations to the debate on states (ahwal). He explains how denying states leads to a threefold classification, clarifies what is actually being denied when one rejects al-ma'nawiyya, and contrasts the Sunni and Mu'tazila views. This lesson helps you navigate a central disagreement in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Understand the threefold division
When states are negated, everything falls into existents, non-existents, and mental considerations. This framework is considered more correct.
Attributes like 'powerful' become mental considerations.
2
Clarify the meaning of denying al-ma'nawiyya
Denying the attributive state does not mean denying the attribute itself, only an intermediary between existent and non-existent.
There is consensus on God being powerful.
3
Compare Sunni and Mu'tazila positions
Ahl al-Sunna attribute power to a real meaning (ma'na) subsisting in the essence; Mu'tazila say 'powerful by essence' as a metaphor.
Mu'tazila deny additional ma'ani but affirm qadiriyya.
4
Note the ruling on denial
Denying qadiriyya leads to unbelief, but denying the ma'ani does not unless opposites are affirmed.
Denying ahwal (states) is considered the truth.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what do the Mu'tazila mean when they say God is 'powerful by His essence'?
A) They affirm that power is an attribute subsisting in God's essence.
B) They deny that God is powerful.
C) They treat power as a metaphor for God's essential ability.
D) They say power is a state between existent and non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The debate on states concerns whether an extra entity mediates between God's essence and His attributes; Ahl al-Sunna ultimately agree on the reality of attributes while the Mu'tazila reinterpret them as essential descriptions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the attribute of God's power, do you tend to think of it as something separate from His essence, or as directly Him? How does this lesson's discussion of states (ahwal) challenge or confirm your assumption? Take a moment to write down what it truly means for God to be 'powerful by His essence' and whether your heart rests in that reality.
?
The Mu'tazila denied that God has separate attributes, yet they affirmed that He is powerful by His essence. Could it be that your own understanding of God's attributes sometimes slips into thinking of them as almost separate 'parts'? Examine your du'a and tawakkul: do you rely on God's power as if it is simply Him, or as if it is something He has? Let this question lead you to deeper tawhid.
?
If denying an intermediary state (al-hal) is the correct position of Ahl al-Sunna, what does that imply about how close God's attributes are to His essence? How should this affect the way you speak about God's mercy or justice in your daily life? Commit to one concrete change in how you describe Allah to your family or friends, based on this lesson.

The Author's Intent and the Distinction of States
Commentator argues against interpreting the author's poem as about states, citing textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why interpreting al-Laqqani's poem as discussing the attributive state is incorrect, and how the distinction between state and mental consideration applies.

Core Idea
The author's own words show he was listing names, not discussing the nature of attributes.

The commentator now addresses a potential misinterpretation of al-Laqqani's poem. Some scholars thought the poem was about the attributive state (al-ma'nawiyya), but the commentator argues that the author's explicit statement and his wording (using 'the Living' instead of 'His being living') show otherwise. He also resolves an objection about al-Sa'd regarding mental considerations.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the commentator's claim
The commentator asserts that al-Laqqani intended only to explain the names of God, not the attributive attributes.
This is based on the author's own statement.
2
Examine the wording evidence
The author used 'al-Hayy' (the Living) instead of 'His being living', supporting the name interpretation.
The proverb 'the owner of the house knows best' reinforces this.
3
Understand the objection about al-Sa'd
Some objected that al-Sa'd considered existence a mental consideration while it is real.
The response is that al-Sa'd did not affirm states, so for him mental considerations can include real things.
4
Appreciate the importance of authorial intent
Correct interpretation requires understanding the author's framework and purpose.
This lesson shows the need to distinguish between state (hal) and mental consideration (i'tibar).

الإلهيات أمر اعتباري انتزاعي؛ لأنه انتزع من الهيئة الثابتة في الخارج، وأمور اعتبارية اختراعية ك (بحر من زئبق) فهو أمرٌ اعتباري اختراعي؛ لأنه اخترعه الشخص، والقسم الأول لا يتوقف على اعتبار المعتبر وفرض الفارض والقسم الثاني يتوقف على ذلك. وعلى القول بنفي الأحوال تكون الأمور ثلاثة: موجودات ومعدومات، وأمور اعتبارية بقسميها، وهذه الطريقة هي الراجحة. ومعنى إنكار المعنوية إنكار زيادتها على المعاني بحيث تكون واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم، لا إنكار كونه قادراً مثلاً من أصله؛ لأنَّه مُجمَعٌ عليه فليس فيه خلافٌ إنما الخلاف في زيادته على المعاني. فالحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على الكون قادراً مثلاً، لكن على القول بثبوت الأحوال تكون واسطة بين الموجود والمعدوم لازمة للقدرة، وعلى القول بنفي الأحوال تكون عبارةٌ عن قيام القدرة بالذات فيكون أمراً اعتباريًا، وهذا كله عند أهل السنة وأما عند المعتزلة فهي كناية عن القادرية (١)؛ أي: كونه قادراً بذاته، وكذا يُقال في الباقي، فهم وإن أنكروا المعاني لم ينكروا القادرية والعالمية وغيرهما، فيقولون: قادر بذاته، وعالم بذاته، إلى غير ذلك؛ ولذلك يقولون: من أنكر المعاني لا يكفر إلا إذا أثبت ضدها، ومن أنكر المعنوية بمعنى القادرية ونحوها كَفَرَ؛ لأنه يلزم من إنكار القادرية إثباتُ الضد، وأما إنكار المعنوية بمعنى الأحوال فهو الحقّ. وحيث علمت أن المصنف صرّح بأنه أراد مجرد بيان الأسماء، ولم يُرِدْ بيان الصفات المعنوية، علمت أن حمله على بيان المعنوية ليس على ما ينبغي، وإن ذكره الشيخ عبد السلام وغيره، خصوصاً وقد عبر بالحي ... إلى آخره، ولم يعبر يكونه حيا .. إلى آخره، وقد قالوا: صاحب البيت أدرى بالذي فيه. للذات، والاعتباري لا يكون قارا للذات كما علمت ويرد على هذا الفرق أن السعد ومن تبعه جعلوا الوجود أمراً اعتباريًا مع كونه قارًا للذات والجواب أن هذه التفرقة عند القائلين بثبوت الأحوال، والظاهر أن السعد ومن تبعه ليسوا من القائلين بثبوت الأحوال، فالأمر الاعتباري عندهم يشمل ما كان قارا للذات، يُؤخذ معظم هذا من حاشية الدسوقي على المصنف، والمراد به (المعظم) الفرق بين الحال والاعتبار فقط. انتهى أجهوري. (١) قوله: (القادرية) هي تمكنه من الإيجاد والإعدام بذاته. انتهى أجهوري.
The divine matters: a mental consideration derived from the state established externally. And there are invented mental considerations like 'a sea of mercury' – it is an invented mental consideration because the person invented it. The first type does not depend on the consideration of the considerer or the supposition of the supposer, while the second type depends on that. According to the negation of states (ahwal), the things are three: existents, non-existents, and mental considerations of both types. This method is the more correct. And the meaning of denying the attributive state (al-ma'nawiyya) is denying its addition to the intelligible meanings (al-ma'ani) such that it would be an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent, not denying that He is 'powerful' for example from its root, because it is agreed upon, so there is no disagreement in it; the disagreement is only in its addition to the meanings. So the summary is that they agreed on the existence of being powerful, for example, but according to the affirmation of states, it is an intermediary between the existent and the non-existent necessary for power; and according to the negation of states, it is an expression for the subsistence of power in the essence, so it becomes a mental consideration. All this is according to Ahl al-Sunna. As for the Mu'tazila, it is a metaphor for power-ability (al-qadiriyya), i.e., His being powerful by His essence. And similarly is said in the rest. So even if they denied the intelligible meanings (al-ma'ani), they did not deny power-ability, knowledge-ability, and others. So they say: 'Powerful by His essence, Knowing by His essence, etc.' Therefore they say: whoever denies the intelligible meanings does not become an unbeliever unless he affirms their opposite; and whoever denies the attributive state in the sense of power-ability and the like becomes an unbeliever, because denying power-ability necessarily entails affirming the opposite. As for denying the attributive state in the sense of states, it is the truth. And since you know that the author explicitly stated that he intended merely the explanation of the names, and did not intend the explanation of the attributive attributes (al-sifat al-ma'nawiyya), you know that interpreting him as explaining the attributive state is not as it should be, even if Shaykh Abd al-Salam and others mentioned it, especially since he expressed with 'the Living... etc.' and did not express 'His being living... etc.' And they said: 'The owner of the house knows best what is in it.' For the essence, and the mental consideration is not established for the essence as you know. And this distinction is objected to by the fact that al-Sa'd and those who followed him made existence a mental consideration while it is established for the essence. The answer is that this distinction is according to those who affirm states, and it appears that al-Sa'd and those who followed him are not among those who affirm states, so the mental consideration according to them includes what is established for the essence. Most of this is taken from the gloss of al-Dasuqi on the author. And what is intended by 'most' is the distinction between the state and the consideration only. End of al-Ujhuri. (1) His saying: 'al-qadiriyya is His ability to bring into existence and annihilate by His essence.' End of al-Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 188

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of mental considerations (umur i'tibariyya) followed by a detailed theological debate regarding the nature of divine attributes, specifically the question of states (ahwal) and the position of Ahl al-Sunna versus the Mu'tazila. The commentator begins by defining a mental consideration as either derived (intiza'i) from an external state or invented (ikhtira'i) by the mind, giving the example of 'a sea of mercury'. The first type does not depend on a subject's consideration, while the second does. On the view that rejects states (ahwal), things are divided into three: existents, non-existents, and mental considerations of both types—this is considered the preferred method. The commentator then clarifies that denying 'al-ma'nawiyya' (the attributive state) means denying that it is an additional entity mediating between existent and non-existent, not denying that God is powerful, etc. The disagreement is about the addition of such states to the meanings (ma'ani), not about the reality of the attribute. He then explains the agreement: all agree that God is powerful, etc., but on the view affirming states, it is an intermediary; on the view negating states, it is merely the subsistence of power in the essence (hence a mental consideration). This is the Sunni position. The Mu'tazila treat these as metaphors: 'powerful by His essence' (qadir bi-dhatihi). They deny the additional intelligible meanings (ma'ani) but affirm the power-ability (qadiriyya) and knowledge-ability (alimiyya). They hold that denying the ma'ani does not entail unbelief unless one affirms their opposites, but denying the qadiriyya does entail unbelief because it necessitates affirming the opposite (i.e., inability). Denying ahwal, however, is considered the truth. The commentator then notes that the author (al-Laqqani) only intended to explain the names of God, not the attributive attributes, so interpreting his poem as discussing the attributive state is incorrect, despite what some scholars like Shaykh Abd al-Salam said. He supports this by noting that the author used 'the Living' (al-Hayy) not 'His being living'. The phrase 'the owner of the house knows best' is a proverb. The passage ends with a discussion about the consistency of mental considerations: some objected that al-Sa'd and his followers considered existence a mental consideration even though it is established for the essence. The reply is that this distinction (between mental and real) applies only for proponents of states, and al-Sa'd is not among them—for him, mental considerations include what is established for essence. This is taken from al-Dasuqi's gloss. The footnote clarifies that 'qadiriyya' means ability to create and annihilate by essence.

Key Terms
المصنف
بيان الأسماء
الصفات المعنوية
السعد
الحال
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the commentator reject the interpretation that the author was explaining the attributive state?
A) Because the author explicitly said he was explaining names.
B) Because the author used the term 'al-Hayy' instead of 'His being living'.
C) Both A and B.
D) Because most scholars agree otherwise.

Key Takeaway
Authorial intent and precise wording are crucial for correct interpretation; al-Laqqani's poem is about listing divine names, not analyzing states.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the names of Allah (e.g., al-Hayy, al-‘Alīm), do you pause to appreciate them as direct names, or do you find yourself slipping into abstract philosophical discussions about how they are realized? What would change if you took al-Laqqānī's approach of focusing solely on the names themselves?
?
The commentator trusts the author's own words over the interpretations of later scholars. In your own learning, do you often rely on secondary explanations without verifying the original intent? How can you adopt the 'owner of the house knows best' principle in your study of the Qur'an and Sunnah?
?
Al-Sa‘d considered existence a mental concept yet still real. Have you ever dismissed a theological concept as 'just mental' or 'not real'—perhaps wrongly? How can you humbly recognize the limits of your own reasoning and trust the clear statements of the scholars who carefully avoid unnecessary speculation?

The Divine Attribute of Life: Essential to God Alone
Commentator defines the attribute of life as essential to God alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute of life is essential to His essence, whereas all created life is contingent and derived.

Core Idea
God alone has life by His essence; all creation receives life from Him.

This lesson introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic theology: God's life is not like ours. The commentator defines the true reality of the Living (al-Hayy) and explains that only God possesses life inherently. This sets the stage for understanding divine attributes as unique and incomparable.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the core meaning.
2
Identify the key distinction
Notice the phrase 'life for His essence' (لذاته) and the contrast with creation whose life is not for their essence. This is the central point.
3
Reflect on the implication for tawhid
Consider why this distinction matters: it shows God's absolute self-sufficiency and uniqueness, reinforcing the oneness of God in His attributes.
This attribute is a foundation for understanding other divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة الحي الذي له الحياة الحقيقية، هو الذي تكون حياته لذاته، وليس ذلك الأحد من الخلق فليس حياتهم لذاتهم.
And the reality of the Living who has true life is He whose life is for His essence, and that is not the case for any of creation, so their life is not for their essence.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of life (al-hayat). The commentator states that the true reality of the Living (al-Hayy) is the one whose life is essential to Himself—i.e., it is not acquired or dependent on anything else. He then distinguishes God from creation: no created being has life that is essential to itself; their life is given and sustained by God. This emphasizes God's absolute self-sufficiency and uniqueness in possessing life inherently.

Key Takeaway
God's attribute of life is unique because it belongs to His very essence, while all other life is a gift from Him.

Definition of the Attribute of Knowledge
Commentator defines God's attribute of knowledge and explains the superlative form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intensive form 'Alim (All-Knowing) refers to the vastness of objects known without implying multiplicity in God's knowledge itself.

Core Idea
Intensified form reflects abundant objects, not multiple knowledges.

The passage examines the divine attribute of knowledge as expressed by the name 'Alim. The commentator explains that while 'Alim and 'Alim share the same meaning, the intensive form is used to highlight the comprehensive scope of God's knowledge. This distinction is crucial to avoid misunderstanding that God's knowledge is multiple or acquired.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text slowly. Notice the structure: it starts with 'قوله: (عَلِيمٌ)' and then explains the meaning. Pay attention to the grammatical notes about the deleted subject and the fa.
2
Understand the literal translation
Read the English translation alongside the Arabic. Compare how 'Alim is translated and how the commentator clarifies the intensive form.
3
Grasp the key doctrinal point
The commentator emphasizes that the intensive form does not imply multiplicity in the attribute itself but in the objects of knowledge. This is because God's knowledge is one and eternal, yet encompasses all things.
This prevents the misconception that there are multiple knowledges.
4
Test your understanding
Take the checkpoint quiz. It directly tests the reason for the intensive form. Answer and read the feedback.
The feedback cites the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (عَلِيمٌ) أي: وحيث وجب له العلم فهو عليمٌ ، فهو خبر لمبتدأ محذوف شأنه مقرون بالفاء كما تقدم، وعليم بمعنى عالم ؛ وهو الذي علمه شامل لكل ما من أن يعلم ؛ فصيغة المبالغة باعتبار الكثرة في المتعلق، وإن كانت صفة العلم واحدةً لا تكثر فيها .
His saying: ('Alim) i.e., and since knowledge is necessarily attributed to Him, He is 'Alim. So it is a predicate of a deleted subject, its matter connected with fa as previously stated. And 'Alim has the meaning of 'Alim (knower); and He is the one whose knowledge encompasses everything that can be known. So the intensive form is due to the multiplicity in the objects, even though the attribute of knowledge is one and does not multiply in it.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of knowledge (al-ilm). The commentator explains that the word 'Alim is used because knowledge is necessarily attributed to God. He notes that 'Alim is the same in meaning as 'Alim (knower), but uses the intensive form to indicate the vastness of the objects of knowledge, not that there are multiple knowledges. The attribute of knowledge itself is one and does not admit of multiplicity; the intensification is only with respect to the objects known (al-muta'alliq). The commentator also clarifies the grammatical role: the word is a predicate (khabar) of an implied subject, linked by fa (as in previous discussions).

Key Takeaway
God's attribute of knowledge is single and infinite; the intensive form 'Alim highlights the limitless scope of what He knows, not any variation in His knowledge itself.

The Divine Attribute of Power: Acting and Refraining
Commentator defines God's power as the ability to act or refrain according to His will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of God's power as the ability to act or refrain according to His will.

Core Idea
God's power includes both the ability to act and to refrain.

The commentator now clarifies the meaning of God's attribute 'Qadir' (All-Powerful). He explains that divine power is not merely the ability to act, but also encompasses the ability to refrain. This balanced definition safeguards against limiting God's power to only one aspect of action.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic definition
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting that the commentator starts by connecting the term 'Qadir' to the necessary attribute of power.
The word 'Qadir' is a predicate of a deleted subject (huwa Qadir).
2
Understand the definition of Qadir
The definition states that Qadir is 'the one who, if He wills, He acts, and if He wills, He refrains.' Recognize the emphasis on volition and capability in both directions.
3
Reflect on the phrase 'mutamakkin min al-fi'l wa al-tark'
Notice the term 'mutamakkin' (fully capable) – it implies that God is equally able to perform or abstain from an action. Both are within His power.
This guards against the notion that God's power is limited to action only.
4
Consider the purpose of action and refraining
The passage concludes that both action and refraining proceed from God according to the benefits of creation. This highlights divine wisdom.
God does not act or refrain arbitrarily but with wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (قادِرُ ) أي : وحيث وجبت له القدرة فهو قادر، فهو خبر لمبتدأ محذوف مقرون بالفاء كما مر والقادرُ : وهو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، فهو متمكن من الفعل والترك، فيصدر عنه كل من الفعل والترك (١) ، بحسب مصالح الخلق المترتبة على ذلك . (١) في (ب): باختياره تعالى).
And His saying: (Qadir) i.e., and since power is necessarily attributed to Him, He is Qadir. So it is a predicate of a deleted subject linked with fa as previously mentioned. And al-Qadir: He is the one who, if He wills, He acts, and if He wills, He refrains. So He is capable of action and refraining, and both action and refraining proceed from Him according to the benefits of creation that are dependent on that. (1) In (b): by His choice, may He be exalted.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of power (al-qudra). The commentator explains that the word Qadir is used because power is necessarily attributed to God. He defines Qadir as the one who has the ability to act or refrain according to His will—He is fully capable (mutamakkin) of both. Both action and inaction proceed from Him based on what benefits His creation. The footnote provides a variant reading (from manuscript 'b') that clarifies that this is 'by His choice' (bi-ikhtiyarihi ta'ala), emphasizing divine volition.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that God's power is limited to action, i.e., He only brings things into existence and cannot refrain from acting.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that God is capable of both action and refraining, and both proceed from Him according to His wisdom. Refraining is also an exercise of power.

Key Takeaway
God's power (qudra) is the ability to both act and refrain, both subject to His will and wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The passage notes that God's power includes the ability to refrain 'by His choice'. Do you truly believe that God is sovereign over His own choices—that He is not forced by your actions or circumstances? How does that challenge your tendency to control or to despair when things go contrary to your plan?

Definition of the Attribute of Will (Murid)
Commentator defines God's will as the attribute that particularizes existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine attribute of will (irada) as the attribute by which Allah particularizes the non-existent with existence.

Core Idea
God's will is what brings possibilities from non-existence into existence.

This lesson examines a concise definition of the divine attribute of will from the commentary on Jawharat al-Tawhid. The author clarifies that God is called Murid because will is necessarily attributed to Him, and He particularizes what was non-existent with existence. Understanding this helps distinguish will from other attributes like power or knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the compact style of the definition.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on the terms Murid, irada, ma'dum, and takhsis. Understand their meanings in this context.
These terms are central to the definition.
3
Grasp the function of will
The passage states that will 'particularizes the non-existent with existence.' Reflect on what this means for creation.
Will is not just a general intention but a specific determination.
4
Note the phrase 'for example'
The commentator uses 'for example' to indicate that will applies to all instances of bringing things from non-existence to existence, not just one case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مُرِيدُ) أي : وحيث وجبت له الإرادة فهو مريد، وهو الذي تتوجه إرادته إلى المعدوم فتخصصه بالوجود بدلاً عن العدم مثلاً .
His saying: (Murid) i.e., and since will is necessarily attributed to Him, He is Murid. And He is the one whose will turns toward the non-existent and particularizes it with existence instead of non-existence, for example.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of will (al-irada). The commentator explains that the word Murid is used because will is necessarily attributed to God. He defines Murid as the one whose will is directed toward the non-existent (al-ma'dum) and particularizes (tukhasissuhu) it with existence rather than non-existence. In other words, God's will is what distinguishes which possibilities come into being and when, as opposed to remaining non-existent. The phrase 'for example' indicates that will applies to all acts of creation and determination.

Key Terms

الإرادة
المعدوم
تخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think will is only about moral choice or whim.
Correction: Here, will is defined as the divine attribute that brings things from non-existence to existence, emphasizing its role in creation.

Checkpoint

A) To make God's power effective.
B) To particularize non-existent things with existence.
C) To know all things.
D) To give life to creatures.

Key Takeaway
God's will is the attribute by which He particularizes non-existent things with existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How does this lesson challenge that assumption? Will you consciously recognize His will in that matter today?
?
If God's will particularizes the non-existent with existence, then nothing exists without His will. How does this affect your sense of control? Are you willing to surrender the illusion that you bring things into existence by your own power, and instead live with constant reliance on His will?
?
The passage says God's will 'turns toward the non-existent'. Think about something you deeply desire that does not yet exist. Are you relying on your own means, or are you turning to the One whose will brings things from non-existence to existence? What concrete step can you take today to demonstrate your trust in His will over your own effort?

Divine Attribute of Hearing Defined
Commentator defines God's attribute of hearing and clarifies poetic truncation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute of hearing is necessary and that the truncated form in the poem is due to poetic meter, not a change in meaning.

Core Idea
God hears all things; a poetic truncation does not alter His attribute.

This lesson examines a line from the Jawharat al-Tawhid poem where the attribute of hearing appears in a shortened form. The commentator explains that this is only due to poetic necessity and affirms that God's hearing is a necessary attribute. Let's look at the passage and its explanation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the translation to understand the basic meaning.
Notice the truncated form 'Sam'' with sukun on 'ayn.
2
Focus on the poetic truncation
The commentator explicitly says the word appears truncated 'due to poetic necessity' (li al-darura). This is a common feature in Arabic poetry to maintain meter.
The meaning remains unchanged.
3
Understand the necessity of hearing
The passage states: 'since hearing is necessarily attributed to Him, He is Sami'.' This affirms that hearing is an essential attribute of God, not accidental.
Necessity (wujub) here means logical necessity.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal point
God hears all things without any instrument or limitation. The poetic truncation does not affect this belief.
This aligns with the broader Sunni creed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (سَمِعْ) بحذف الياء مع تسكين العين للضرورة؛ أي: وحيث وجب له السمع فهو سميع .
And His saying: (Sami') with the deletion of the ya and the sukun on the 'ayn due to poetic necessity; i.e., and since hearing is necessarily attributed to Him, He is Sami'.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage is a definition of the divine attribute of hearing (al-sam'). The commentator notes that the word Sami' appears in the poem with a truncated form (Sami') due to poetic meter (darura), where the ya is dropped and the 'ayn is given sukun. He explains that the meaning is the same: because hearing is necessarily attributed to God, He is described as Sami' (All-Hearing). The definition implies that God hears all things without any instrument or limitation.

Key Terms

السمع
ضرورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The truncated form 'Sam'' might be mistaken for a different word or imply a limitation in God's hearing.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the truncation is purely due to poetic necessity and does not change the meaning; God remains All-Hearing without any limitation.

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a different word.
B) Due to poetic necessity (darura).

Key Takeaway
God's attribute of hearing is necessary and affirmed, regardless of poetic form.

The Attribute of Sight: Al-Basir's All-Encompassing Vision
Sanusi defines God's sight as encompassing all visible things without distraction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defines the divine attribute of sight (al-Basir) as encompassing all visible things without distraction.

Core Idea
God's sight encompasses all things simultaneously without any distraction.

In this passage, the commentator al-Sanūsī explains the meaning of 'al-Basir' in relation to God. He connects it with the attribute of hearing (al-Sami') and emphasizes that God's perception is perfect and all-encompassing. This definition guards against any notion of limitation or sequential perception in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic carefully, noting the structure: 'وقوله بَصِيرٌ' then the definition.
Pay attention to the phrase 'من غير أن يشغله شأن عن شأن'.
2
Understand the parallel definitions
Notice that the commentator defines both 'Sami’' and 'Basir' in relation to all existents, using 'يحيط' (encompasses).
3

This links to the broader lesson on divine attributes.

The key point is that God's perception is perfect and simultaneous—nothing occupies Him from anything else. This affirms that God is all-hearing and all-seeing without any limitation or distraction.

God's Life: Essential vs. Accidental Life
Al-Bajuri defines God's life as essential and distinguishes it from creatures' accidental life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's attribute of life is essential to His essence, while the life of creatures is merely granted and accidental.

Core Idea
God's life is self-subsistent; creatures' life is borrowed.

Al-Bajuri now turns to the attribute of life, one of the essential divine attributes. He gives a concise definition that cuts to the heart of tawhid: what it means for God to be 'the Living' in a way that no creature shares. The distinction is not about duration but about the very nature of life itself—whether it proceeds from the essence or is received from another.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, focusing on the contrast between 'life for His own essence' and 'not for their own essences'.
Notice the particle 'ليس' which negates the possibility for creatures.
2
Identify the core distinction
Identify the two key phrases: 'حياته لذاته' and 'ليس حياتهم لذاتهم'. Understand that this is a qualitative, not quantitative, difference.
3
Reflect on implications
Consider why this distinction matters: if God's life were accidental like ours, He would depend on something else, contradicting His divinity.
This connects to the principle of necessity (wujub) in divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحقيقة الحي الذي له الحياة الحقيقية، هو الذي تكون حياته لذاته، وليس ذلك الأحد من الخلق فليس حياتهم لذاتهم.
And the reality of the Living who has true life is that His life is for His own essence, and that is not the case for any of the creatures, for their life is not for their own essences.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute 'al-Hayy' (the Living) for God. It explains that true life is self-subsistent life that belongs to the essence itself, not derived from another. Creatures possess life only by God's grant; their life is accidental, not essential. This underscores God's absolute self-sufficiency and the difference between Creator and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Attribute 'Alim: One Knowledge, Many Objects
Al-Bajuri defines the attribute 'Alim and explains the reason for the intensive form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of Allah's attribute of being Knowing (al-'Alim) and why the intensive form is used.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is one, but its objects are many.

In this passage, al-Bajuri clarifies the attribute 'Alim (Knowing). He explains that just as Allah necessarily possesses knowledge, He is described as 'Alim. Moreover, he addresses a subtle point: why does the Arabic use the intensive form 'Alim rather than the simple 'Adil? The answer lies not in the attribute itself but in the vastness of what is known.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the grammatical construction and the key statement about the intensive form.
The passage uses a technical style; focus on the last part about 'صيغة المبالغة'.
2
Identify the main definition
Find where al-Bajuri says 'وعليم بمعنى عالم' and note that 'Alim means 'World'—one whose knowledge encompasses everything knowable.
3
Understand the intensive form
The passage explicitly states that the intensive form is due to the multiplicity of objects (الكثرة في المتعلق), not because the attribute itself is multiple.
Contrast with the misconception that the intensive form implies multiplicity in God's knowledge.
4
Reflect on theological significance
Why is it important that God's knowledge is one? Consider how this protects tawhid and avoids attributing composition to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (عَلِيمٌ) أي: وحيث وجب له العلم فهو عليمٌ ، فهو خبر لمبتدأ محذوف شأنه مقرون بالفاء كما تقدم، وعليم بمعنى عالم ؛ وهو الذي علمه شامل لكل ما من أن يعلم ؛ فصيغة المبالغة باعتبار الكثرة في المتعلق، وإن كانت صفة العلم واحدةً لا تكثر فيها .
His saying: ('Alim) means: since knowledge is necessary for Him, He is 'Alim, so it is a predicate of an omitted subject connected with 'fa' as previously. And 'Alim is in the meaning of 'World; and He whose knowledge encompasses everything that can be known. And the intensive form is due to the multiplicity of objects, even though the attribute of knowledge is one and does not admit multiplicity.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute 'Alim (Knowing). It notes the grammatical construction: 'Alim is a predicate of an omitted subject (huwa) connected by 'fa'. It clarifies that 'Alim is synonymous with 'World, meaning that God's knowledge encompasses all knowable things. The intensive form (مبالغة) is used because the objects of knowledge are many, not because the attribute itself is multiple; the attribute of knowledge is a single, unmultiplied attribute.

Key Terms
علم
عالم
عليم
صيغة المبالغة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intensive form 'Alim implies that God's knowledge is multiple or has degrees.
Correction: The intensive form is due to the multiplicity of objects, not any multiplicity in the attribute itself.

Checkpoint
Why is the intensive form 'Alim used rather than just 'World?
A) To indicate that God's knowledge is greater than human knowledge
B) Because the objects of God's knowledge are many
C) To emphasize that God knows the future
D) Because the attribute of knowledge is multiple

Key Takeaway
The intensive form of 'Alim emphasizes the vastness of what God knows, not any multiplicity in His knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that God's knowledge is one yet encompasses everything. Does your heart truly rest in the certainty that He knows every detail of your life—your struggles, your secret sins, your unspoken needs—even when you feel unseen by others? What concrete step can you take today to live in that awareness and surrender your anxieties to Him?
?

?
The intensive form 'Alim points to the vastness of what God knows, not any lack in His knowledge. Are there areas of your life where you doubt that God's knowledge is sufficient? Confess one such area and resolve to respond with trust rather than fear.

Definition of the Powerful (al-Qadir) Attribute
Al-Bajuri defines the Powerful attribute and explains its scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Qadir and why divine power includes both the ability to act and the ability to refrain.

Core Idea
Divine power includes both the ability to act and the ability to refrain.

Al-Bajuri continues defining the essential attributes of Allah. After establishing power as an attribute, he now defines the one who possesses it, al-Qadir. This lesson clarifies that God's power is not limited to action but also includes the ability to leave actions undone, according to His wisdom and the interests of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then its literal translation. Notice the grammatical structure: 'fa-huwa Qadir' as a predicate of an omitted subject.
The pattern matches previous attribute definitions in the text.
2
Understand the definition
The definition states: 'He is the one who, if He wills, He does, and if He wills, He leaves.' This means God's power enables both action and refraining. Ask yourself why both are necessary for perfect power.
3
Reflect on the scope
The passage concludes that both action and refraining issue from God 'according to the interests of creation.' This shows that God's exercise of power is wise and purposeful, not arbitrary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (قادِرُ ) أي : وحيث وجبت له القدرة فهو قادر، فهو خبر لمبتدأ محذوف مقرون بالفاء كما مر والقادرُ : وهو الذي إن شاء فعل وإن شاء ترك، فهو متمكن من الفعل والترك، فيصدر عنه كل من الفعل والترك (١) ، بحسب مصالح الخلق المترتبة على ذلك .

And al-Qadir: He is the one who, if He wills, He does, and if He wills, He leaves; thus He is capable of both acting and refraining, so both action and refraining issue from Him, according to the interests of creation that are dependent on that.
Source pages p. 189

Commentary
This passage defines the attribute Qadir (Powerful). It follows the same grammatical pattern as the previous attributes. The definition emphasizes that God's power includes both the ability to act and the ability to refrain from acting. This refutes the view that God necessarily acts; He is free to do or not do. The exercise of power is according to His wisdom and the interests of creation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

3

The Hearing Attribute and Poetic License
Al-Bajuri defines the Hearing attribute with a poetic license.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Allah has the attribute of Hearing (Sami') and why the poem uses a shortened form for poetic meter.

Core Idea
Allah's Hearing is eternal; poetic meter doesn't change the attribute.

The poem 'Jawharat al-Tawhid' uses compact language. Sometimes words are shortened to fit the meter. In this line, the word 'Sami'' is written as 'Sami'' with a dropped ya. This lesson explains the attribute and the poetic necessity, clarifying that the shortening does not affect the doctrine.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic line carefully, noting the missing ya and the sukun on 'ayn. Then read the literal translation to understand the meaning.
The text explicitly mentions 'ضرورة' (poetic necessity).
2
Understand the attribute and the poetic license
Reflect on the fact that the attribute of hearing is necessarily true of Allah, and the poetic shortening is merely a textual adjustment for meter, not a theological change.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : (سَمِعْ) بحذف الياء مع تسكين العين للضرورة؛ أي: وحيث وجب له السمع فهو سميع .
And His saying: (Sami') with the ya dropped and the 'ayn made sukun due to necessity; meaning: since hearing is necessary for Him, He is Sami'.

Allah's Seeing and Hearing encompass all existence without distraction.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains the meaning of Allah's attribute al-Basir (the Seeing). He also clarifies the relationship between hearing and seeing, emphasizing that both attributes involve comprehensive and simultaneous perception of all existents. This teaching guards against imagining divine perception as limited or subject to distraction.

1
Read the passage and translation

Pay attention to the phrase 'يحيط بالمسموعات والمبصرات'.

Commentary

Both attributes indicate comprehensive perception: God hears all existent sounds and sees all existent sights. Importantly, His perception is not limited by distraction; He perceives everything simultaneously without one thing occupying Him from another. This underscores God's absolute perfection.

A) They are limited to certain things

Allah's seeing and hearing are attributes of perfect, comprehensive, and simultaneous perception, free from any distraction or limitation.

The Unity of God's Will and Wish
Al-Bajuri explains that God's will and wish are one attribute, refuting the Karramiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ash'ari majority holds that God's will (irada) and wish (mashiyya) are one and the same eternal attribute, and how this differs from the Karramiyya view.

Core Idea
God's wish and will are identical; distinguishing them is a mistake.

In this passage, al-Bajuri explains a phrase from al-Laqqani's poem that touches on a key debate about God's attributes. The majority Sunni position is that God's will (irada) and His wish (mashiyya) are not two separate attributes but one and the same. The Karramiyya sect, however, claimed otherwise. Let's examine the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the poetic shortening of 'yasha' for meter. Then read the translation to grasp the meaning.
2
Understand the majority position
The passage states that the author (al-Laqqani) adopts the majority view: unity of mashiyya and irada. This means they are one attribute, not two.
3
Contrast with the Karramiyya view
The Karramiyya claimed that mashiyya is eternal and singular, while irada is temporal and multiple. The passage refutes this distinction.
4
Connect to God's shu'un
The passage ends by linking God's intentions (iradat) to His shu'un (states/affairs) in creation, showing that God's will is expressed through His acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (مَا يَشَا يُرِيدُ) بقصر (يشا) للوزن؛ أي: الذي يشاؤه يريده، وأشار المصنف بذلك إلى اختيار مذهب الجمهور من اتحاد المشيئة والإرادة، خلافاً للكرامية حيث زعموا أن المشيئة صفةٌ واحدةٌ أزلية تتناول ما يشاؤه الله بها والإرادة حادثة متعددة بتعدد المرادات كما قاله في شرحه الصغيره(1)، ومراداته تعالى : هي شؤونه (2) في خلقه.
His saying: 'What He wills He intends' with shortening of 'yasha' for meter; meaning: what He wills, He intends. And by this the author pointed to adopting the position of the majority regarding the unity of wish (mashiyya) and will (irada), contrary to the Karramiyya who claimed that wish is a single eternal attribute that encompasses what God wishes by it, and that will is temporal and multiple according to the multiplicity of objects willed, as he said in his small commentary. And His intentions, exalted is He, are His states (shu'un) in His creation.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage explains the phrase 'ما يشا يريد' from the poem. The author (al-Laqqani) indicates the majority Ash'ari position that mashiyya (wish) and irada (will) are one and the same eternal attribute. The Karramiyya sect incorrectly distinguished them, claiming mashiyya is eternal and irada is temporal. The passage also connects God's intentions (iradat) with His 'shu'un' (states/affairs) in creation, meaning that God's will is expressed through His acts in creation.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The majority view is that they are one and the same eternal attribute, as stated in the passage: 'اتحاد المشيئة والإرادة'.

Checkpoint

C) They are one and the same attribute

Key Takeaway
The unity of mashiyya and irada confirms that God's will is one eternal attribute, undivided and consistent with His simplicity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Are you willing to accept the hard parts of His will as equally from His one perfect desire for you? What step can you take today to submit more fully to that one will?
