واعلم أنَّ للسمع والبصر والإدراك على القول به والقول بأنَّه يتعلق بكل موجود ثلاث تعلقات : تعلقاً تنجيزيا قديماً : وهو التعلق بذات الله وصفاته. وصلوحيًّا قديماً : وهو التعلق بنا قبل وجودنا . وتنجيزيا حادثاً : وهو التعلق بنا بعد وجودنا . التعلُّق لهذه الصفات مُستفاد من صيغة الأمر في قوله : (أنظ)، كما ووجوب استفيد عدم تناهي متعلقاتها من أداة العموم الداخلة على (موجود)، وسكت المصنف عن وحدة هذه الصفات للعلم بها من وجوبها لنظائرها ؛ كالقدرة والإرادة؛ إذ لا فرق. ولا إيطاء في كلام المصنف لاختلاف مرجع ،الضميرين، نظير ما تقدَّم في اسمي الإشارة في قوله : (ومثل ذي إرادة .. إلى آخره وسبق ما في نحوه ۳۷ - وَغَيْرُ عِلْمٍ هَذِهِ كَمَا نَبَتْ ثُمَّ الحَيَاةُ مَا بِشَيْ نَعَلَّقَتْ . قوله : (وَغَيْرُ عِلْمٍ هذِهِ أي : هذه الصفاتِ الأربع ـ وهي : الكلام، والسمع والبصر، والإدراك - غير العلم، فاسم الإشارة مبتدأ مؤخَّر، و (غير علم خبر مقدَّم، ودفع بذلك ما قد يتوهم من اتحادها مع العلم لاتحاد متعلق الكلام مع متعلّق العلم، واندراج متعلّق السمع والبصر والإدراك في متعلقه، لا سيما وتعلق هذه الثلاثة تعلق انكشاف كتعلَّقِ العلم، وكما أنَّ هذه الصفاتِ الأربع مغايرة للعلم بعضُها مغاير لبعض، واتحاد المتعلّق لا يوجب اتحاد الحقيقة. وقوله : كَمَا ثَبَتْ) أي: كالتغاير الذي ثبت عند القوم بالأدلة السمعيَّة؛ لأنَّ هذه الصفاتِ إِنَّما ثبتت بالسمع والمدلول لغةٌ لكلِّ واحدةٍ غيرُ المدلول للأُخرى، فوجب حمل ما ورد على ظاهره حتى يثبت خلافُهُ ، وبيان كون المدلول لغة لكلّ واحدة غير المدلول للأخرى، أنَّ السمعَ حِسُّ الأُذُنِ؛ أي حاستها، والأُذُنَ نفسها، وما وقر فيها من شيء تسمعه والذكر المسموع والبصرَ حِسُّ العين؛ أي حاستها، والكلام القولُ، وما كان مكتفياً بنفسه، والعلم : هو المعرفة كما يُؤخذ من «القاموس» في مواضع متعددة، وإذا ثبت أنها متغايرة لغةً كانت متغايرةً شرعاً، وبالجملة: فكنه كل واحدة غير كنه الأخرى، ونفوض علم ذلك الله تعالى.
Know that for hearing, sight, and perception, on the opinion that it exists and the opinion that it relates to every existent, there are three attachments: an eternal actual attachment: which is the attachment to the essence of Allah and His attributes; an eternal potential attachment: which is the attachment to us before our existence; and a contingent actual attachment: which is the attachment to us after our existence. The attachment for these attributes is derived from the imperative form in His saying (anẓur), just as the obligation and the non-limitation of their objects is derived from the generalizing particle that enters upon (mawjūd). The author remains silent about the unity of these attributes because it is known from their necessity to their counterparts, like power and will; for there is no difference. And there is no redundancy in the author's speech due to the difference of the referent of the two pronouns, analogous to what was said earlier regarding the two demonstrative pronouns in his saying (wa mithl dhī irāda ... etc.). And what preceded regarding its like. 37 - AND OTHER THAN KNOWLEDGE, THESE AS IT IS ESTABLISHED? THEN LIFE, IT IS NOT ATTACHED TO ANYTHING. His saying: (AND OTHER THAN KNOWLEDGE, THESE) i.e., these four attributes — which are speech, hearing, sight, and perception — are other than knowledge. So the demonstrative pronoun is a delayed subject, and (other than knowledge) is an advanced predicate. By this he repels what might be imagined of their unity with knowledge due to the unity of the object of speech with the object of knowledge, and the inclusion of the objects of hearing, sight, and perception in its object, especially since the attachment of these three is a disclosure attachment like the attachment of knowledge. And just as these four attributes are distinct from knowledge, some of them are distinct from others; and the unity of the object does not necessitate the unity of the essence. And his saying: (AS IT IS ESTABLISHED) i.e., like the distinctness that is established among the people by scriptural proofs; because these attributes are only established by scripture, and the linguistic denotation of each one is other than the linguistic denotation of the other. So it is necessary to take what is reported in its literal sense until its contrary is established. The explanation that the linguistic denotation of each is other than the linguistic denotation of the other: that hearing is the sense of the ear, i.e., its faculty, and the ear itself, and what is placed in it of something one hears, and the memory of what is heard; and sight is the sense of the eye, i.e., its faculty; and speech is the saying, and what is self-sufficient; and knowledge is cognition, as taken from al-Qamus in multiple places. And if it is established that they are linguistically distinct, then they are theologically distinct. In summary, the essence of each one is other than the essence of the other, and we consign the knowledge of that to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage does two things. First, it enumerates the three types of attachment (talluq) for the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and perception (on the opinion that they attach to all existents): eternal actual (to God's essence and attributes), eternal potential (to us before we exist), and contingent actual (to us after we exist). It notes that the imperative form 'anẓur' (look/consider) implies these attachments, and the generalizing particle implies their objects are unlimited. Then the commentator explains that the author omitted discussion of the unity of these attributes because it is known by analogy with power and will. Second, the passage comments on the line 'Other than knowledge, these as it is established' and launches into an argument that these four attributes (speech, hearing, sight, perception) are distinct from knowledge and from each other. The potential confusion arises because they share the same object (all existents) and have a similar 'disclosure' nature. The commentator refutes this by invoking linguistic distinctions: in language, each term refers to a different sense or concept (e.g., hearing is ear-sense, sight is eye-sense, speech is utterance, knowledge is cognition). Since scripture establishes these attributes, we must take them in their literal sense, and thus they are distinct theologically. The passage concludes that the true essence of each is known only to Allah.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attributes of speech, hearing, sight, and perception are identical to the attribute of knowledge because they all have the same object (every existent) and involve disclosure.
Correction: The passage argues that unity of object does not imply unity of essence. Linguistic distinctions prove they are separate attributes.

Key Takeaway
Although hearing, sight, speech, and perception share the same object as knowledge, they are distinct attributes because each term has a different linguistic meaning, and essence is not determined by object.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah's attributes like 'All-Hearing' and 'All-Seeing', do you ever reduce them to mere concepts in your mind? How will you now train yourself to remember that their true essence is beyond your comprehension, yet they are truly distinct and real?
?
This lesson shows that you cannot fully grasp the how of Allah's attributes. Does that make you uncomfortable? How will you cultivate a heart that worships a God whose attributes are known only to Him, yet accepts them as they are?
?
Your natural tendency is to assume that what you understand is complete. How will you remind yourself daily that Allah's attributes are not limited to your conceptual boxes, and that this very limitation is a sign of His majesty?

Divine Attributes

The Three Attachments of Hearing, Sight, and Perception
Classification of the three attachments of hearing, sight, and perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of attachments (ta'alluqāt) for the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and perception, and how they are derived from scripture.

Core Idea
Divine attributes attach in three modes: eternal-determining, eternal-potential, and originated-determining.

This lesson opens the discussion on the relational aspects of the divine attributes. The author explains that even for attributes like hearing and sight, the way they 'attach' to their objects varies depending on whether the object is the divine essence, pre-creation beings, or post-creation beings. Understanding this tripartite classification is crucial for grasping how eternal attributes relate to contingent things without implying change in Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the three distinct types of attachment mentioned.
2
Identify the three attachments
List the three attachments: (1) t'anjiizi qadim, (2) suluhi qadim, (3) t'anjiizi hadith. Understand the object of each.
The first attaches to Allah's essence and attributes, the second to us before existence, the third to us after existence.
3
Note the scriptural derivation
The author derives attachment from the imperative 'Look' (anẓur) and the universality from the particle in 'every existent'. Observe how Qur'anic expressions ground these concepts.
4
Understand why unity is not mentioned
The author remained silent about the unity of these attributes because it is already known from their necessity for similar attributes like power and will.
This indicates shared underlying principles across attributes.

Know that hearing, sight, and perception – according to the view that holds them and the view that they attach to every existent – have three attachments: (1) a determining attachment, eternal: which is the attachment to the Essence of Allah and His attributes. (2) a potential attachment, eternal: which is the attachment to us before our existence. (3) a determining attachment, originated: which is the attachment to us after our existence. The attachment for these attributes is derived from the imperative form in His statement: 'Look' (anẓur); similarly, the necessity of the non-finiteness of their objects is derived from the universal particle entering upon 'existent' (mawjūd). The author remained silent about the unity of these attributes because it is known from their necessity for their analogues, like power and will, for there is no difference. There is no repetition in the author’s speech due to the different reference of the two pronouns, analogous to what preceded regarding the two demonstrative pronouns in his statement: 'and like the one possessing will...' and what is similar preceded. 37 – 'And other than knowledge these, as established.' His statement: 'And other than knowledge these' means: these four attributes – namely speech, hearing, sight, and perception – are other than knowledge. So the demonstrative pronoun is a delayed subject, and 'other than knowledge' is a forwarded predicate. By that he repels what might be imagined of their unity with knowledge due to the unity of the object of speech with the object of knowledge, and the inclusion of the objects of hearing, sight, and perception in its object, especially since the attachment of these three is an attachment of disclosure like the attachment of knowledge. And just as these four attributes are distinct from knowledge, some of them are distinct from others, and the unity of the object does not necessitate unity of essence. His statement: 'as established' i.e. like the distinctness that is established among the people by scriptural proofs, because these attributes are only established by scripture, and the linguistic denotation for each one is other than the denotation for the other. So it is obligatory to interpret what is reported according to its apparent meaning until its contrary is established. The explanation that the linguistic denotation for each is other than the denotation for the other is that hearing (al-sam‘) is the sense of the ear, i.e. its faculty, and the ear itself, and what settles in it of something it hears, and the heard remembrance; sight (al-baṣar) is the sense of the eye, i.e. its faculty; speech (al-kalām) is utterance, and what is self-sufficient; and knowledge (al-‘ilm) is cognition, as taken from al-Qāmūs in multiple places. And when it is established that they are distinct linguistically, they are distinct legally. In summary: the reality of each one is other than the reality of the other, and we consign knowledge of that to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage provides a classification and theological clarification regarding the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and perception (al-idrāk). The author first states that these three attributes have three types of attachment (ta‘alluqāt): (1) a determining and eternal attachment to Allah's Essence and attributes themselves, (2) a potential attachment to us before we exist, and (3) a determining but temporal attachment to us after we exist. The authority for these attachments is derived from the Qur’anic imperative 'Look' (Sūrat al-Mulk, 67:19) and the universal particle in the expression 'every existent' which indicates that their objects are unlimited. The author notes that the original text (al-Jawhara) does not explicitly mention the unity of these attributes because it is already known from their necessity for similar attributes like power and will. He then explains line 37 of the poem, which states that these four attributes (speech, hearing, sight, perception) are distinct from knowledge. This is important because some might think they are identical to knowledge since they share objects. The author refutes this by pointing out that the object of speech is not identical to the object of knowledge (since speech is the self-sufficient utterance) and that the objects of hearing, sight, and perception are included in knowledge only in a general sense. He emphasizes that unity of object does not imply unity of essence. Finally, he provides a linguistic argument: each attribute has its own distinct meaning in the Arabic language – hearing is the sense of the ear, sight the sense of the eye, speech is utterance, knowledge is cognition – as given in lexicons. Since these are distinct linguistically, they are distinct in religious usage. The ultimate reality of each is beyond human grasp, and we consign its full knowledge to Allah.

These Attributes Are Distinct from Knowledge
Argument that hearing, sight, perception, and speech are distinct from knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hearing, sight, perception, and speech are distinct from knowledge, and why sharing objects does not imply unity of essence.

Core Idea
Distinct linguistic meanings prove the attributes are distinct, even if their objects overlap.

This lesson addresses a potential confusion: if speech, hearing, sight, and perception relate to the same realities as knowledge, one might think they are identical. The author clarifies that each attribute has a unique linguistic definition, and unity of object does not imply unity of essence. The language itself shows they are different, and we accept this as established by scripture.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'وَغَيْرُ عِلْمٍ هَذِهِ' to the end. Focus on the argument that these attributes are other than knowledge.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that these four attributes (speech, hearing, sight, perception) are distinct from knowledge. Find the phrase that states this.
The phrase is 'وَغَيْرُ عِلْمٍ هَذِهِ' meaning 'and other than knowledge these'.
3
Understand the potential confusion
The confusion arises because some attributes share objects with knowledge. The author explains that unity of object does not necessitate unity of essence.
For example, speech and knowledge both relate to the same thing, but they are different in reality.
4
Analyze the linguistic evidence
The author provides dictionary meanings: hearing is the sense of the ear, sight is the sense of the eye, speech is utterance, knowledge is cognition. These are distinct in language, hence distinct in religion.
5
Conclude with tafwid
The author ends by consigning the full reality of each attribute to Allah. This reflects the Ahl al-Sunna approach of affirming attributes without delving into how.

Know that hearing, sight, and perception – according to the view that holds them and the view that they attach to every existent – have three attachments: (1) a determining attachment, eternal: which is the attachment to the Essence of Allah and His attributes. (2) a potential attachment, eternal: which is the attachment to us before our existence. (3) a determining attachment, originated: which is the attachment to us after our existence. The attachment for these attributes is derived from the imperative form in His statement: 'Look' (anẓur); similarly, the necessity of the non-finiteness of their objects is derived from the universal particle entering upon 'existent' (mawjūd). The author remained silent about the unity of these attributes because it is known from their necessity for their analogues, like power and will, for there is no difference. There is no repetition in the author’s speech due to the different reference of the two pronouns, analogous to what preceded regarding the two demonstrative pronouns in his statement: 'and like the one possessing will...' and what is similar preceded. 37 – 'And other than knowledge these, as established.' His statement: 'And other than knowledge these' means: these four attributes – namely speech, hearing, sight, and perception – are other than knowledge. So the demonstrative pronoun is a delayed subject, and 'other than knowledge' is a forwarded predicate. By that he repels what might be imagined of their unity with knowledge due to the unity of the object of speech with the object of knowledge, and the inclusion of the objects of hearing, sight, and perception in its object, especially since the attachment of these three is an attachment of disclosure like the attachment of knowledge. And just as these four attributes are distinct from knowledge, some of them are distinct from others, and the unity of the object does not necessitate unity of essence. His statement: 'as established' i.e. like the distinctness that is established among the people by scriptural proofs, because these attributes are only established by scripture, and the linguistic denotation for each one is other than the denotation for the other. So it is obligatory to interpret what is reported according to its apparent meaning until its contrary is established. The explanation that the linguistic denotation for each is other than the denotation for the other is that hearing (al-sam‘) is the sense of the ear, i.e. its faculty, and the ear itself, and what settles in it of something it hears, and the heard remembrance; sight (al-baṣar) is the sense of the eye, i.e. its faculty; speech (al-kalām) is utterance, and what is self-sufficient; and knowledge (al-‘ilm) is cognition, as taken from al-Qāmūs in multiple places. And when it is established that they are distinct linguistically, they are distinct legally. In summary: the reality of each one is other than the reality of the other, and we consign knowledge of that to Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 201

Commentary
This passage provides a classification and theological clarification regarding the divine attributes of hearing, sight, and perception (al-idrāk). The author first states that these three attributes have three types of attachment (ta‘alluqāt): (1) a determining and eternal attachment to Allah's Essence and attributes themselves, (2) a potential attachment to us before we exist, and (3) a determining but temporal attachment to us after we exist. The authority for these attachments is derived from the Qur’anic imperative 'Look' (Sūrat al-Mulk, 67:19) and the universal particle in the expression 'every existent' which indicates that their objects are unlimited. The author notes that the original text (al-Jawhara) does not explicitly mention the unity of these attributes because it is already known from their necessity for similar attributes like power and will. He then explains line 37 of the poem, which states that these four attributes (speech, hearing, sight, perception) are distinct from knowledge. This is important because some might think they are identical to knowledge since they share objects. The author refutes this by pointing out that the object of speech is not identical to the object of knowledge (since speech is the self-sufficient utterance) and that the objects of hearing, sight, and perception are included in knowledge only in a general sense. He emphasizes that unity of object does not imply unity of essence. Finally, he provides a linguistic argument: each attribute has its own distinct meaning in the Arabic language – hearing is the sense of the ear, sight the sense of the eye, speech is utterance, knowledge is cognition – as given in lexicons. Since these are distinct linguistically, they are distinct in religious usage. The ultimate reality of each is beyond human grasp, and we consign its full knowledge to Allah.

Misconception: Some may think that because hearing, sight, and perception involve 'disclosure' of objects, they are the same as knowledge.
Correction: The passage states that despite the similarity in attachment (disclosure), they remain distinct due to different linguistic denotations and the principle that unity of object does not necessitate unity of essence.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Hearing, sight, perception, and speech are distinct from knowledge because each has a unique linguistic meaning, and unity of object does not require unity of essence.

?
If your own knowledge and speech were identical, you could never truly communicate with others. How does realizing that Allah's attributes are distinct deepen your understanding of His perfection as the All-Hearing, All-Seeing, All-Knowing, and Speaking? What specific change will you make in your du'a (supplication) because of this?

The Divine Attribute of Life: Not Relational, but Qualifying
Explains that life is a necessary condition for perception and has no attachment to an object.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the attribute of life (al-ḥayāh) is not a relational attribute but a qualifying attribute that enables perception.

Core Idea
Life enables perception; it does not attach to an object.

In this passage, the commentator explains a crucial distinction: some divine attributes, like knowledge and will, relate to objects outside themselves. But life is different. It is a condition for perception without having an external attachment. Let's explore how the author unpacks this verse line and why life, along with existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness, falls into a special category.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic line and translation
Start by reading the verse line and its translation. Notice the key term 'tataʿallaq' (attaches).
The line uses poetic license with vowel elision.
2
Understand 'thing' in two senses
The commentator explains that 'thing' can mean either existent or non-existent, linguistically, or technically. But life attaches to neither.
This broader sense ensures the argument covers all possibilities.
3
Distinguish relational vs. qualifying attributes
Relational attributes (like knowledge) require an object. Qualifying attributes (like life) enable other attributes and do not require an external relation.
Life is a muṣaḥḥiḥa – it makes its subject capable of perception.
4
Note the similar attributes
Existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness are also qualifying attributes according to some scholars. They do not attach to anything beyond the subject.
This groups life with other essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ الحَيَاةُ مَا بِشَيْ تَعَلَّقَتْ) بسكون الياء وحذف الهمزة للوزن، وثم للاستئناف، والمعنى: أن الحياة لا تتعلَّق بشيءٍ؛ أي أمر موجود أو معدوم، فالمراد بالشيء هنا المعنى اللغوي الشامل للموجود والمعدوم، ويصح أن يكون المراد به المعنى الاصطلاحي، ويُقال: إذا كانت لا تتعلَّق بالموجود، فأولى ألا تتعلق بالمعدوم، فليست الحياة من الصفات المتعلقة؛ لأنَّها صفةٌ مُصَحْحَةٌ للإدراك؛ أي: مُصَحِّحَةُ لمن قامت به أن يتّصف بصفات الإدراك، ولا تقتضي أمراً زائداً على قيامها بمحلها، ومثلُ الحياةِ الوجود والقدم والبقاء عند من يعدُّها من الصفات الذاتية.
His statement: 'Then life does not attach to anything' – with the yā’ vowelless and elision of the hamza for meter; ‘then’ (thumma) is for resumption. The meaning is that life does not attach to anything, i.e., to any existent or non-existent thing. So what is meant by ‘thing’ here is its linguistic sense that encompasses the existent and the non-existent. It is also valid that what is meant is the technical sense. And it is said: if it does not attach to the existent, it is even more fitting that it does not attach to the non-existent. Thus life is not among the relational attributes, because it is a qualifying attribute (muṣaḥḥiḥa) for perception; i.e., it qualifies the one in whom it subsists to be described by the attributes of perception. It does not require anything additional beyond its subsistence in its locus. Similar to life are existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness according to those who count them among the essential attributes.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage explains the verse line about the attribute of life (al-ḥayāh). The author states that life does not attach to any object, neither existing nor non-existing. The term 'thing' (shay’) here is taken in its broad linguistic sense covering both existence and non-existence. He argues that since life has no attachment to the existent, it certainly has none to the non-existent. Therefore life is not a ‘relational attribute’ (ṣifa muta‘alliqa) like knowledge or power; instead it is a ‘qualifying attribute’ (ṣifa muṣaḥḥiḥa) that enables the entity in which it subsists to possess the attributes of perception. Life does not demand any extra relation beyond its own subsistence in the subject. The author concludes by mentioning that similar to life are existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness for those who consider them essential attributes, meaning they are not relational but rather conditions for other attributes.

Correction: The passage clarifies that life is a precondition for perception and does not require an object; it is a qualifying attribute, not a relational one.

Checkpoint

B) Because it only attaches to the existent.

The Eternity of Allah's Names and the Refutation of the Mu'tazilite Objection
Affirms the eternity of Allah's names and refutes the objection that names are created words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ash‘ari scholars affirm that Allah's names are eternal despite being words, and how they answer the Mu‘tazilite objection.

Core Idea
Allah's names are eternal as divine designations, not as uttered sounds.

This passage tackles a key theological dispute: if Allah's names are Arabic words, which are created, how can they be eternal? The response sharpens the distinction between the name as a word and the divine act of naming (tasmiya), showing that the names are eternal in terms of Allah's eternal intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get the flow of the argument.
Notice the objection and the two-stage response.
2
Identify the key parties and their claims
Note that the Mu‘tazila say names are originated (created) and human inventions; the Ash‘aris affirm they are eternal.
3
Trace the objection and answer structure
Find the objection (names are words → created) and the initial answer (eternal not in themselves but in tasmiya). Then see the further critique and its resolution.
The footnote from al-Ujhūrī provides the final answer.
4
Grasp the concept of tasmiya
Understand that tasmiya refers to Allah's eternal intention to be designated by those words in the future. The placing of the name does not depend on utterance.
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسماء الله تعالى وصفاته قديمة قوله : (وَعِنْدَنَا أَسْمَاؤُهُ العَظِيمَة كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ قَدِيمَة) لما فرغ من الصفات وتعلقاتها شرع في مبحث يجب اعتقاده، فيجب على الإنسان أن يعتقد أنَّ أسماءه العظيمة قديمة، وكذا صفات ذاته، وتقديم الظرف للحصر، والضمير لأهل الحقِّ، فالمعنى: وأسماؤه العظيمة قديمة عندنا معاشر أهل الحقِّ، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم : بأنَّ أسماءه تعالى حادثة، وأنَّها من وضع الخلق . واستُشكل الأول بأنَّ الأسماء ألفاظ، وهي حادثة قطعاً، فتكون الأسماء حادثة قطعاً، فكيف توصف الأسماء بالقدم؟ وأجيب بأنّها قديمة لا باعتبار ذاتها، بل باعتبار التسمية بها . وبُحث في هذا الجواب (١) بأن التسمية وضع الاسم للمسمّى، وحيث كان الاسم حادثاً كانت التسمية حادثة؟ (1) قوله : ( وبحث هذا الجواب هذا البحث غير ظاهر ; لأنا نقول : التسمية هي وضع الاسم، وهو قصد الله أزلاً أن تكون الألفاظ الموجودة في علمه دالّة عليه فيما لا يزال، وحينئذ لا يلزم من حدوث الاسم حدوث وضعه ; لأن وضع الاسم لا يتوقف على النطق به. انتهى أجهوري.
The names of Allah Almighty and His attributes are eternal. His statement: 'And with us His great names, likewise the attributes of His Essence, are eternal.' When he finished with the attributes and their attachments, he began a topic that must be believed. So it is obligatory for a person to believe that His great names are eternal, and likewise the attributes of His Essence. The prepositional phrase is fronted for restriction, and the pronoun refers to the people of truth. So the meaning is: His great names are eternal with us, the community of the people of truth, contrary to the Mu‘tazila in their statement that His names are originated and that they are from the invention of creation. It was objected to the first that names are words, and they are certainly originated, so the names are certainly originated. How then can names be described as eternal? And it was answered that they are eternal not with regard to their essences, but with regard to being named (al-tasmiya) by them. And this answer was examined (1) saying that naming is the placing of the name for the named, and since the name is originated, the naming is originated? (1) His statement: 'this answer was examined' – this examination is not apparent; for we say: naming is the placing of the name, and it is Allah's eternal intention that the words existing in His knowledge should indicate Him in the future. In that case, the origination of the name does not necessitate the origination of its placing, because the placing of the name does not depend on uttering it. End of al-Ujhūrī.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage addresses the belief that Allah's names are eternal, in opposition to the Mu‘tazila who considered them originated (ḥāditha) and products of human invention. The author quotes the line of the poem asserting that both the names and the attributes of the Essence are eternal. An objection is raised: names are words (alfāẓ) and words are created, so how can they be described as eternal? The initial answer is that the names are eternal not in their own essences (i.e., not as uttered sounds) but in terms of 'being named' (tasmiya), meaning the eternal intention of Allah to be designated by those names. A further critique is then examined: if naming is placing a name for a named, and the name itself is created, then isn't the naming also created? The footnote (1) from al-Ujhūrī responds: naming (tasmiya) is Allah's eternal intention (qaṣd) that the words existing in His knowledge (i.e., the eternal archetypes of the names) will indicate Him in the future when they are used. The act of placing the name does not depend on vocal utterance; hence the origination of the actual spoken name does not contradict the eternity of the divine intention to be named. This preserves the belief that Allah's names are eternal in a meaningful sense.

وضع الاسم
معتزلة

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The Ash‘aris distinguish between the name as a word (created) and the naming (tasmiya) as Allah's eternal intention to be designated by those words, which is what is meant by the names being eternal.

D) They accept that names are created but still call them eternal metaphorically.

Key Takeaway
Allah's names are eternal because they are rooted in His eternal intention to be designated by those words, not in the contingent uttered sounds.

The Attribute of Life: Non-Relational and Qualifying
Commentary explains that life does not relate to any object and is a qualifying attribute for perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the attribute of life in Allah is a non-relational, qualifying attribute that does not relate to any object, and its role in enabling perception.

Core Idea
Life is a qualifying attribute, not a relational one.

In studying divine attributes, a key distinction emerges: some attributes relate to objects (like power or will), while others do not. The attribute of life is one of the latter. In this passage, the commentator explains why life is not relational and why it is considered a qualifying attribute that enables perception. Understanding this distinction deepens our grasp of Allah's essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the commentator's opening explanation of the poetic line and the meaning of 'life is not related to anything.'
The Arabic uses poetic license with sukun and elision.
2
Identify the key claim
Focus on the central claim: life does not relate to any object, whether existent or non-existent. Understand the linguistic scope of 'thing' here.
The commentator clarifies that 'thing' includes both existent and non-existent in the linguistic sense.
3
Follow the logical argument
Notice the reasoning: if life does not relate to the existent, it is even more so not related to the non-existent. This leads to the conclusion that life is not a relational attribute.
4
Grasp the concept of a qualifying attribute
The commentator explains that life is a qualifying attribute (ṣifa muṣaḥḥiḥa) – it qualifies the essence to possess attributes of perception. It does not require anything beyond its own subsistence.
This contrasts with relational attributes like power, which require an object.
5
Note similar attributes
The passage lists existence, pre-eternity, and everlastingness as similar non-relational attributes according to some scholars. Recognize that these also do not relate to objects.
This helps generalize the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ثُمَّ الحَيَاةُ مَا بِشَيْ تَعَلَّقَتْ) بسكون الياء وحذف الهمزة للوزن، وثم للاستئناف، والمعنى: أن الحياة لا تتعلَّق بشيءٍ؛ أي أمر موجود أو معدوم، فالمراد بالشيء هنا المعنى اللغوي الشامل للموجود والمعدوم، ويصح أن يكون المراد به المعنى الاصطلاحي، ويُقال: إذا كانت لا تتعلَّق بالموجود، فأولى ألا تتعلق بالمعدوم، فليست الحياة من الصفات المتعلقة؛ لأنَّها صفةٌ مُصَحْحَةٌ للإدراك؛ أي: مُصَحِّحَةُ لمن قامت به أن يتّصف بصفات الإدراك، ولا تقتضي أمراً زائداً على قيامها بمحلها، ومثلُ الحياةِ الوجود والقدم والبقاء عند من يعدُّها من الصفات الذاتية .
His saying: "Then life is not related to anything" with sukun on the ya and elision of the hamza for meter, and 'thumma' for resumption. The meaning: that life is not related to anything; that is, any existing or non‑existent thing. So the intended meaning of 'thing' here is the linguistic meaning encompassing the existent and the non‑existent, and it is valid that the intended meaning be the technical meaning. And it is said: if it is not related to the existent, then it is even more appropriate that it is not related to the non‑existent. Thus life is not among the relational attributes; because it is a qualifying attribute for perception; that is, it qualifies the one in whom it inheres to be described with attributes of perception, and it does not require anything additional beyond its subsisting in its locus. And similar to life are existence, pre‑eternity, and everlastingness according to those who count them among the essential attributes.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a line from Jawharat al-Tawhid concerning the divine attribute of life (al‑ḥayāh). The commentator clarifies that the line means life does not relate (tataʿallaq) to any object, whether existent or non‑existent. The word 'thing' here is taken in its broad linguistic sense, not specifically the technical sense of a 'thing' (al‑shayʾ) as an existent. He then argues that if life does not relate to the existent, it is even more so not related to the non‑existent. Therefore, life is not one of the relational attributes (al‑ṣifāt al‑mutaʿalliqa) like will or power; rather it is a qualifying attribute (ṣifa muṣaḥḥiḥa) that enables the one in whom it subsists to possess attributes of perception (such as knowledge, will, etc.). Life does not require anything beyond its own subsistence in the essence. The commentator also notes that existence (al‑wujūd), pre‑eternity (al‑qidam), and everlastingness (al‑baqāʾ) are similar to life in this respect, according to those who consider them essential attributes (ṣifāt dhātiyya).

The Eternity of Allah's Names and the First Objection
Declaration of the eternity of Allah's names and response to objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Ahl al-Haqq affirm the eternity of Allah's names despite the createdness of uttered words, and the distinction between the name as a linguistic entity and as a reference to the divine essence.

Core Idea
Allah's names are eternal in meaning, not in their uttered letters.

This lesson tackles a critical theological and philosophical question: if Allah's names are words we speak, and words are created, how can the names be eternal? The commentator presents the orthodox position and directly answers an objection from the Mu'tazila, clarifying a key distinction between the verbal form and the eternal significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the declaration of belief
Read the opening statement where the commentator declares that Allah's names are eternal, and notice how he contrasts it with the Mu'tazila position.
Pay attention to the nuance of the restriction phrase.
2
Examine the objection
Understand the objection raised: if names are words and words are created, how can they be eternal? This is a serious challenge.
3
Study the answer
The response distinguishes between the name's essence (the uttered word) and the naming (the reference). This is the key insight.
Reflect on how this distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أسماء الله تعالى وصفاته قديمة
۳۸ - وَعِنْدَنَا أَسْمَاؤُهُ العَظِيمَة كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ قَدِيمَة
قوله : (وَعِنْدَنَا ... إلى آخره) لما فرغ من الصفات وتعلقاتها شرع في مبحث يجب اعتقاده، فيجب على الإنسان أن يعتقد أنَّ أسماءه العظيمة قديمة، وكذا صفات ذاته، وتقديم الظرف للحصر، والضمير لأهل الحقِّ، فالمعنى: وأسماؤه العظيمة قديمة عندنا معاشر أهل الحقِّ، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم : بأنَّ أسماءه تعالى حادثة، وأنَّها من وضع الخلق .
واستُشكل الأول بأنَّ الأسماء ألفاظ، وهي حادثة قطعاً، فتكون الأسماء حادثة قطعاً، فكيف توصف الأسماء بالقدم؟
وأجيب بأنّها قديمة لا باعتبار ذاتها، بل باعتبار التسمية بها .
The names of Allah Most High and His attributes are eternal.
38 – "And according to us, His magnificent names, likewise the attributes of His essence, are eternal."
His saying: (waʿindanā ... etc.) – When he finished with the attributes and their relations, he began a topic that must be believed. So it is obligatory for a person to believe that His magnificent names are eternal, and likewise the attributes of His essence. The fronting of the adverb is for restriction, and the pronoun refers to the people of truth. So the meaning: His magnificent names are eternal according to us, the community of Ahl al‑Ḥaqq, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their statement that His names are originated and that they are from the creation of creatures.
And the first [view] was objected to on the grounds that names are utterances, and they are unequivocally originated, so the names are unequivocally originated; so how can the names be described as eternal?
And it was answered that they are eternal not with respect to their essence, but with respect to the naming by them.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal declaration and the first objection‑answer pair concerning the eternity of Allah's names. The commentator states that it is obligatory to believe that Allah's names and attributes are eternal (qadīma), and this is the position of Ahl al‑Ḥaqq (the people of truth), as opposed to the Muʿtazila who claim that the names are created (ḥāditha) and invented by creatures. Then an objection is raised: names are utterances (alfāẓ), which are undeniably created, so how can they be eternal? The answer given is that they are eternal not in their own essence (bi iʿtibār dhātihā) but by virtue of the naming (al‑tasmiya) — that is, the act of naming by which they refer to the Eternal. This separates the verbal form from the eternal significance.

Key Terms
قديم
الأسماء
التسمية
المعتزلة
حادثة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah's names are eternal in their physical utterance as words.
Correction: They are eternal only in the sense of the naming (al-tasmiya), i.e., their reference to the eternal essence, not in their uttered form.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, how are Allah's names said to be eternal despite being uttered words?
A) By virtue of their letters being uncreated
B) By virtue of the naming (al‑tasmiya), not their verbal essence
C) Because they are written in the Preserved Tablet
D) Because the Muʿtazila also affirm their eternity

Key Takeaway
The names of Allah are declared eternal by Ahl al-Haqq, not because the uttered words are pre-eternal, but because they signify the eternal divine essence through the act of naming.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the name 'Allah' or 'Ar-Rahman', do you feel the weight of eternity – that this utterance connects you to something that has always been and will always be? Or do the words become familiar and empty? Commit today to pause on each name and silently affirm: 'This name is eternal, pointing to the Eternal One.'
?
The Mu'tazila reduced Allah's names to human inventions – a product of culture. Have you ever, even subconsciously, treated God’s attributes as mere concepts limited by your understanding? What would it mean for your worship if you truly believed every attribute you ascribe to Him is anchored in His eternal reality, not your imagination?
?
If the names of Allah are eternal because of the act of naming (tasmīyah), then your very invocation of His names is a participation in that eternal reality. Does your tongue honor that truth? When you call upon Allah, do you approach it as a mundane act, or as a profound engagement with what is timeless? Resolve to begin each supplication with a breath of awe.

Objection to the First Answer and the Worthiness Answer
Objection to the first answer and introduction of the worthiness answer with its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection to the claim that names are eternal by virtue of naming, and the alternative answer based on Allah's eternal worthiness, including its limitations against the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Names are eternal because Allah is worthy of them, but this answer fails against the Mu'tazila.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the first answer (that names are eternal because the naming act is eternal) and then offers a second answer: the names are eternal because Allah is eternally worthy of them. However, this answer is quickly critiqued for not refuting the Mu'tazila claim that names are created by humans. Let's examine the text closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection to the first answer
Notice that the objection argues that if the name (ism) is originated, the naming act (tasmiya) must also be originated. This challenges the first answer.
The marginal note by al-Ujhuri provides a defense, but in the main text we only have the objection.
2
Read the second answer: worthiness
The text switches to another answer: the names are eternal because Allah is eternally worthy (sālih) of them. This is a different approach.
The term 'salahiyya' is key.
3
Understand the critique of the worthiness answer
The text points out that this answer does not conflict with the Mu'tazila position, so it is ineffective as a refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبُحث في هذا الجواب (۱) بأن التسمية وضع الاسم للمسمّى، وحيث كان الاسم حادثاً كانت التسمية حادثة؟
(۱) قوله : ( وبحث هذا الجواب هذا البحث غير ظاهر ؛ لأنا نقول : التسمية هي وضع الاسم، وهو قصد الله أزلاً أن تكون الألفاظ الموجودة في علمه دالّة عليه فيما لا يزال، وحينئذ لا يلزم من حدوث الاسم حدوث وضعه ؛ لأن وضع الاسم لا يتوقف على النطق به. انتهى أجهوري.
تحفة المريد على جوهرة التوحيد
وأجيب بأنَّ معنى قدمها : أنَّ الله صالح لها ،ازلاً، فهي قديمة باعتبار الصلاحية.
(1)
وفيه أن هذا لا يحسن في الردّ على المعتزلة الذين يقولون : إنَّها من وضع الخلق، إذ لا ينافيه وبعضهم أجاب بأن قدمها من حيث علم الله تعالى وتقديره في الأزل، وفيه أن جميع الحوادث كذلك، وقيل: إنَّ قدمها من حيث مدلولها ، وفيه أنَّ قدم المدلول يرجع لما سبق من قدم الذات والصفات ولا يحسنُ في الرد على المعتزلة ونقل العلامة الملوي عن سيدي محمد بن عبد الله العربي : فيما سبق أنَّ من كلام الله القديم أسماء له هي المحكوم عليها بالقدم، كما أن منه أمراً ونهياً ... إلى آخره)، وعلى هذا فالمراد بالتسمية القديمة دلالة الكلام أزلاً على معاني الأسماء من غير تبعيض ولا تجزئة في الكلام، وهو الذي ينشرح له الصدر، ولا يردُ أنَّهم لم يذكروا من أقسام الكلام الاعتبارية الأسماء القديمة؛ لأنَّ تقسيمهم ليس حاصراً، بل اقتصروا على الأهم باعتبار ما ظهر لهم كيف ومدلوله لا يدخل تحت حصر ؟!
T
الأولية
وأشار العلامة الملوي في آخر عبارته إلى أن القدم هنا ليس بمعنى عدم بل بمعنى أنَّها موضوعةُ قبل الخلق، فهي من وضعه تعالى قبل خلقه، ثم ألهمها للنور المحمدي، ثم للملائكة، ثم للخلق، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم : بأنَّها من وضع البشر، وفي هذا الكلام تسليمُ أنَّ الأسماء ليست أزليَّةٌ كما لا يخفى.
ه . (۲)
وبالجملة : فهذا المبحث لم يَصْفُ
ونُقل عن القرطبي : أنَّ مَنْ قال: الاسم مشتق من السمو وهو العلو، يقول: لم يزل الله موصوفاً (۳) قبل وجود الخلق وعند وجودهم وبعد فنائهم لا تأثير لهم أسمائه، وهذا قول أهل السنة.
في
ومن قال : الاسم مشتق من السمة، يقول: كان في الأزل بلا أسماء
(۱) قوله : ( فيما سبق ) أي : فيما سبق نقله عنهم، وهو أن أسماءه تعالى حادثة، وأنها من وضع الخلق. انتهى أجهوري.
(۲) قوله : (فهذا البحث لم يصفُ) مبني على البحث في الجواب الأول ؛ وهو أنها قديمة باعتبار التسمية، وقد علمت مما ذكر في الهامش أنه غير وارد. انتهى أجهوري.
(۳) قوله : (موصوفاً) أي : مسمّى بأسمائه انتهى أجهوري.
And it was investigated in this answer (1) that the naming is the placing of the name for the named, and since the name is originated, the naming is originated?
(1) His saying: (And it was investigated in this answer) – this investigation is not apparent; for we say: the naming is the placing of the name, and it is Allah's eternal intention that the utterances existing in His knowledge indicate Him in the future, and then the origination of the name does not necessitate the origination of its placing; because the placing of the name does not depend on uttering it. – End of al-Ujhuri.
Tuhfat al-Mureed on Jawharat al-Tawhid
And it was answered that the meaning of its eternity is that Allah is worthy of it eternally, so it is eternal in terms of worthiness.
(1)
But it is objected that this does not avail against the Muʿtazila who say that they are from the creation of creatures, since it does not contradict it. And some answered that its eternity is by virtue of Allah's knowledge and His predestination in pre‑eternity, but then all originated things are likewise. And it was said that its eternity is by virtue of its meaning, but then the eternity of the meaning goes back to what preceded of the eternity of the essence and attributes, and it does not avail against the Muʿtazila. And al‑Mallawi transmitted from Sayyidī Muḥammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh al‑ʿArabī: in what preceded, that from Allah's eternal speech are names for Him that are judged to be eternal, just as from it are command and prohibition ... etc.), and upon this, the intended meaning of the eternal naming is the indication of speech eternally to the meanings of the names without division or separation in speech. This is what expands the chest, and it is not refuted that they did not mention among the intellectual divisions of speech the eternal names; because their division is not exhaustive, rather they restricted themselves to the most important according to what appeared to them. How can it be when its meaning cannot be confined?!
T
Al‑awwaliyyah
And al‑Mallawi indicated at the end of his statement that eternity here is not in the sense of lack of beginning, but in the sense that they are placed before creation, so they are from His placing before His creation, then He inspired them to the Muhammadan Light, then to the angels, then to the creatures, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their statement that they are from the placement of humans. In this speech there is an admission that the names are not eternal, as is not hidden.
Ha. (2)
In summary: This topic is not clear.
And it was transmitted from al‑Qurṭubī: that whoever says the name is derived from al‑sumuww (highness) says: Allah has never ceased to be described (3) before the existence of creatures, at their existence, and after their annihilation – no effect on His names from them. And this is the statement of Ahl al‑Sunnah.
In it, and whoever says the name is derived from al‑simah (mark) says: He was in pre‑eternity without names.
(1) His saying: (in what preceded) i.e., in what preceded of his transmission from them, which is that His names are originated and that they are from the creation of creatures. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
(2) His saying: (So this topic is not clear) – built upon the investigation of the first answer, which is that they are eternal by virtue of the naming, and you have known from what was mentioned in the margin that it is invalid. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
(3) His saying: (described) i.e., named by His names. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a continuous discussion of objections and further answers concerning the eternity of Allah's names. It begins by critically examining the first answer (that names are eternal by virtue of the naming). The critique argues that if the name is originated, then the naming act (taṣmiya) is also originated. A marginal note from al‑Ujhuri counters that the naming is Allah's eternal intention, not dependent on uttering. Then the main text presents an alternative answer: the names are eternal because Allah is worthy of them (ṣāliḥ) eternally. However, this is deemed insufficient against the Muʿtazila because it does not refute their claim that names are created by humans. Another answer says eternity is by virtue of Allah's knowledge in pre‑eternity, but this would apply to all created things. Another says it is by virtue of the meaning (madlūl), but that goes back to the eternity of the essence and attributes. The commentator then transmits from al‑Mallawi that the names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalām), as command and prohibition are, and that the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of the names without division. This is praised as satisfying. Al‑Mallawi further clarifies that 'eternity' here means they are placed before creation, not that they are without beginning. Finally, two etymologies of 'ism' (name) are presented: from sumuww (highness) implying eternal description, and from simah (mark) implying that in pre‑eternity there were no names. The first is the view of Ahl al‑Sunnah.

Key Terms
التسمية
الصلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any answer about the eternity of names is equally valid.
Correction: The passage shows that the worthiness answer is insufficient against the Mu'tazila; a stronger answer is needed.

Checkpoint
Why is the worthiness answer considered insufficient against the Mu'tazila?
A) It does not explain how names can be eternal.
B) It does not contradict the Mu'tazila claim that names are created by humans.
C) It relies on a false etymology.
D) It implies names are not truly eternal.

Key Takeaway
The worthiness answer redefines the eternity of names but is insufficient against the Mu'tazila because it does not refute their claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The worthiness answer seemed like a solid defense, yet it failed to refute the Mu'tazila. In what areas of your own faith do you rely on arguments that feel convincing but might not withstand scrutiny? What will you do to ensure your beliefs are grounded in revelation, not just clever reasoning?
?
You call upon Allah by His names daily. Have you ever considered the possibility that these names might be human conventions, as the Mu'tazila claim? If that thought unsettles you, what concrete evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah can you produce to affirm their eternity? Commit to finding and memorizing one proof today.

intermediate
4 min
Further Answers: Knowledge, Meaning, and the Eternal Speech View
Examination of knowledge and meaning answers, then presentation of the eternal speech answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative answers that the eternity of names is based on Allah's knowledge or the meaning of names, and the transition to the preferred answer that names are part of Allah's eternal speech.

Core Idea
Names are eternal because they are part of Allah's eternal speech, not merely because of knowledge or meaning.

The text continues to explore answers to the problem of the eternity of names. Some say they are eternal by virtue of Allah's knowledge and predestination, but this applies to all created things. Others say by virtue of their meaning, but that reduces to the eternity of attributes. Finally, al-Mallawi transmits a view that names are part of Allah's eternal speech, which is more satisfying.

Guided Path
1
Read the knowledge-based answer
Note that the answer claims names are eternal because Allah knew them and predestined them in pre-eternity.
The critique is that this applies to all created things, not just names.
2
Read the meaning-based answer
The answer claims names are eternal because their meanings (madlul) are eternal, but the critique is that this reduces to the eternity of the essence and attributes.
3
Read the preferred answer from al-Mallawi
Al-Mallawi transmits that names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalam), like command and prohibition. Thus, the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of names without division.
This is presented as the satisfying answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The commentator then transmits from al‑Mallawi that the names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalām), as command and prohibition are, and that the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of the names without division. This is praised as satisfying. Al‑Mallawi further clarifies that 'eternity' here means they are placed before creation, not that they are without beginning. Finally, two etymologies of 'ism' (name) are presented: from sumuww (highness) implying eternal description, and from simah (mark) implying that in pre‑eternity there were no names. The first is the view of Ahl al‑Sunnah.

Key Terms
علم الله
المدلول
الكلام القديم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Mallawi, what is the 'eternal naming'?
A) The act of naming in pre-eternity
B) The eternal indication of speech to the meanings of names
C) The names themselves being eternal
D) The worthiness of Allah

Key Takeaway
The most satisfying answer is that names are eternal as part of Allah's eternal speech, indicating their meanings without division.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Since Allah's names are part of His eternal speech, how does that change the way you call upon Him by His names? Are you merely reciting words or engaging with an eternal reality?
?
The knowledge answer seemed sufficient but was rejected for being too general. Have you ever settled for a shallow understanding of Allah's attributes when a deeper one is available? What will you do to seek that depth?
?
If Allah's speech includes command and prohibition, and His names are part of that speech, how should that affect your obedience to His commands? Are you treating His commands as temporal or eternal?

The Meaning of 'Eternity' and the Two Etymologies of 'Ism'
Clarification of the meaning of 'eternity' and the two etymologies of 'ism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'eternity' here means pre-creation placement, not absolute uncreatedness, and the two etymologies of 'ism' leading to different views on whether Allah had names in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Names are 'eternal' in the sense of being placed before creation, and the etymology of 'ism' determines whether Allah is named eternally.

This passage clarifies the meaning of 'eternity' (qidam) in this context: it does not mean uncreatedness in time, but rather that the names were placed by Allah before creation. Then it presents two etymologies of the word 'ism' (name): from 'sumuww' (highness) implying eternal description, and from 'simah' (mark) implying that in pre-eternity there were no names. The first is claimed as the view of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Mallawi's clarification of 'eternity'
Note that 'eternity' here is not the usual philosophical meaning of lacking a beginning, but rather 'placed before creation'.
This implies the names are not absolutely eternal in the sense of being without beginning.
2
Read the two etymologies of 'ism'
The first etymology from 'sumuww' (highness) leads to the view that Allah has always been described by His names. The second from 'simah' (mark) leads to the view that in pre-eternity there were no names.
The author states the first is the view of Ahl al-Sunnah.

And it was investigated in this answer (1) that the naming is the placing of the name for the named, and since the name is originated, the naming is originated?
(1) His saying: (And it was investigated in this answer) – this investigation is not apparent; for we say: the naming is the placing of the name, and it is Allah's eternal intention that the utterances existing in His knowledge indicate Him in the future, and then the origination of the name does not necessitate the origination of its placing; because the placing of the name does not depend on uttering it. – End of al-Ujhuri.
Tuhfat al-Mureed on Jawharat al-Tawhid
And it was answered that the meaning of its eternity is that Allah is worthy of it eternally, so it is eternal in terms of worthiness.
(1)
But it is objected that this does not avail against the Muʿtazila who say that they are from the creation of creatures, since it does not contradict it. And some answered that its eternity is by virtue of Allah's knowledge and His predestination in pre‑eternity, but then all originated things are likewise. And it was said that its eternity is by virtue of its meaning, but then the eternity of the meaning goes back to what preceded of the eternity of the essence and attributes, and it does not avail against the Muʿtazila. And al‑Mallawi transmitted from Sayyidī Muḥammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh al‑ʿArabī: in what preceded, that from Allah's eternal speech are names for Him that are judged to be eternal, just as from it are command and prohibition ... etc.), and upon this, the intended meaning of the eternal naming is the indication of speech eternally to the meanings of the names without division or separation in speech. This is what expands the chest, and it is not refuted that they did not mention among the intellectual divisions of speech the eternal names; because their division is not exhaustive, rather they restricted themselves to the most important according to what appeared to them. How can it be when its meaning cannot be confined?!

Al‑awwaliyyah
And al‑Mallawi indicated at the end of his statement that eternity here is not in the sense of lack of beginning, but in the sense that they are placed before creation, so they are from His placing before His creation, then He inspired them to the Muhammadan Light, then to the angels, then to the creatures, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their statement that they are from the placement of humans. In this speech there is an admission that the names are not eternal, as is not hidden.

In summary: This topic is not clear.
And it was transmitted from al‑Qurṭubī: that whoever says the name is derived from al‑sumuww (highness) says: Allah has never ceased to be described (3) before the existence of creatures, at their existence, and after their annihilation – no effect on His names from them. And this is the statement of Ahl al‑Sunnah.
In it, and whoever says the name is derived from al‑simah (mark) says: He was in pre‑eternity without names.
(1) His saying: (in what preceded) i.e., in what preceded of his transmission from them, which is that His names are originated and that they are from the creation of creatures. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
(2) His saying: (So this topic is not clear) – built upon the investigation of the first answer, which is that they are eternal by virtue of the naming, and you have known from what was mentioned in the margin that it is invalid. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
(3) His saying: (described) i.e., named by His names. – End of al‑Ujhuri.

Commentary
This passage is a continuous discussion of objections and further answers concerning the eternity of Allah's names. It begins by critically examining the first answer (that names are eternal by virtue of the naming). The critique argues that if the name is originated, then the naming act (taṣmiya) is also originated. A marginal note from al‑Ujhuri counters that the naming is Allah's eternal intention, not dependent on uttering. Then the main text presents an alternative answer: the names are eternal because Allah is worthy of them (ṣāliḥ) eternally. However, this is deemed insufficient against the Muʿtazila because it does not refute their claim that names are created by humans. Another answer says eternity is by virtue of Allah's knowledge in pre‑eternity, but this would apply to all created things. Another says it is by virtue of the meaning (madlūl), but that goes back to the eternity of the essence and attributes. The commentator then transmits from al‑Mallawi that the names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalām), as command and prohibition are, and that the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of the names without division. This is praised as satisfying. Al‑Mallawi further clarifies that 'eternity' here means they are placed before creation, not that they are without beginning. Finally, two etymologies of 'ism' (name) are presented: from sumuww (highness) implying eternal description, and from simah (mark) implying that in pre‑eternity there were no names. The first is the view of Ahl al‑Sunnah.

The First Two Answers and Their Refutations
Presentation and critique of two answers on the pre-eternity of divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial attempts to explain the pre-eternity of divine names and why they fail to refute the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity as suitability or as knowledge fails to address the Mu'tazila claim that names are human inventions.

The Mu'tazila argue that divine names are created because they are human inventions. Scholars propose various meanings for the pre-eternity of names. In this passage, the author presents two early answers—pre-eternity through 'suitability' and through divine knowledge—and shows their insufficiency. You will see why these answers do not satisfy the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for this lesson, focusing on the first two answers. Notice the structure: first the suitability answer, then its critique, then the knowledge answer, then its critique.
2
Identify the two answers
The passage gives two distinct ways to argue that the names are pre-eternal: (a) because Allah is suitable for them, and (b) because of His eternal knowledge. Write down the reasoning for each.
3
Understand the critique
For each answer, the author points out a flaw. The suitability answer does not refute the Mu'tazila because it does not contradict their claim. The knowledge answer applies to all contingent things, so it doesn't distinguish the names. Summarize these critiques.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Why is it difficult to prove that the names themselves (as words) are pre-eternal? The Mu'tazila view them as human products. These answers try to shift the meaning of pre-eternity. Consider what alternative approaches might work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأن معنى قدمها : أن الله صالح لها أزلاً، فهي قديمة باعتبار الصلاحية. وفيه أن هذا لا يحسن في الرد على المعتزلة الذين يقولون : إنها من وضع الخلق، إذ لا ينافيه وبعضهم أجاب بأن قدمها من حيث علم الله تعالى وتقديره في الأزل، وفيه أن جميع الحوادث كذلك، وقيل: إن قدمها من حيث مدلولها ، وفيه أن قدم المدلول يرجع لما سبق من قدم الذات والصفات ولا يحسن في الرد على المعتزلة ونقل العلامة الملوي عن سيدي محمد بن عبد الله العربي : فيما سبق أن من كلام الله القديم أسماء له هي المحكوم عليها بالقدم، كما أن منه أمراً ونهياً ... إلى آخره)، وعلى هذا فالمراد بالتسمية القديمة دلالة الكلام أزلاً على معاني الأسماء من غير تبعيض ولا تجزئة في الكلام، وهو الذي ينشرح له الصدر، ولا يرد أنهم لم يذكروا من أقسام الكلام الاعتبارية الأسماء القديمة؛ لأن تقسيمهم ليس حاصراً، بل اقتصروا على الأهم باعتبار ما ظهر لهم كيف ومدلوله لا يدخل تحت حصر؟ الأولية وأشار العلامة الملوي في آخر عبارته إلى أن القدم هنا ليس بمعنى عدم بل بمعنى أنها موضوعة قبل الخلق، فهي من وضعه تعالى قبل خلقه، ثم ألهمها للنور المحمدي، ثم للملائكة، ثم للخلق، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم : بأنها من وضع البشر، وفي هذا الكلام تسليم أن الأسماء ليست أزلية كما لا يخفى. وبالجملة : فهذا المبحث لم يصف
And it was answered that the meaning of its pre-eternity is that Allah is suitable for it eternally, so it is pre-eternal in consideration of suitability. But there is in this that it does not improve in refuting the Mu'tazila who say that they are from the creation of the creatures, since it does not contradict it. And some of them answered that its pre-eternity is from the perspective of Allah's knowledge and decree in pre-eternity, but there is in this that all contingent things are likewise. And it was said: its pre-eternity is from the perspective of its signification, but there is in this that the pre-eternity of the signification returns to what preceded of the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes, and it does not improve in refuting the Mu'tazila. And al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī transmitted from Sayyidī Muḥammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh al-ʿArabī: concerning what preceded, that from the pre-eternal speech of Allah are names for Him that are judged to be pre-eternal, just as from it are command and prohibition ... to the end. And upon this, what is intended by the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech upon the meanings of the names without division or fragmentation in speech, and this is what the heart opens to. And it is not objected that they did not mention from the notional divisions of speech the pre-eternal names, because their division is not exhaustive; rather they confined themselves to the most important in consideration of what appeared to them, how, and its signification is not encompassable?! The primacy: And al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī indicated at the end of his expression that pre-eternity here is not in the sense of non-existence but in the sense that they are placed before creation, so they are from His placing, exalted, before His creation, then He inspired them to the Muḥammadan light, then to the angels, then to the creation, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their saying that they are from the placement of humans, and in this speech there is an admission that the names are not eternal, as is not hidden. And in summary: this topic has not become clear.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a set of responses to the objection that the divine names are created. It begins by presenting an answer: the pre-eternity of the names means that Allah is eternally suitable for them, so they are pre-eternal in terms of suitability. However, this answer is criticized as insufficient against the Mu'tazila who claim the names are human inventions. A second answer says their pre-eternity is from Allah's eternal knowledge and decree, but this applies to all contingent things. A third says it is from their signification, but this reduces to the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes. The author then transmits a better answer from al-Mallawi: from Allah's eternal speech are names judged pre-eternal, just as commands and prohibitions are. Thus, the eternal naming means that speech eternally indicates the meanings of the names without division. This is the preferred answer. Al-Mallawi further clarifies that pre-eternity here means the names were placed by Allah before creation and then inspired to the Muhammadan light, angels, and creation, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The author concludes that this topic is not settled.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
صلاحية (salāhiyya)
أزل (azal)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pre-eternity of divine names can be established by saying Allah is suitable for them or He knew them eternally.
Correction: These explanations are not refutations of the Mu'tazila claim that the names are human inventions; they avoid the issue or apply to all contingent things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with the first answer that Allah's suitability makes the names pre-eternal?
A) It denies that names are created.
B) It does not contradict the Mu'tazila claim that names are human inventions.
C) It makes all creation pre-eternal.
D) It is not based on the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The early answers to the Mu'tazila objection fail because they shift the meaning of pre-eternity without directly addressing the claim that names are human inventions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever settled for a theological explanation that felt tidy but left you with a lingering unease—like the answers in this passage? What was it, and why did you accept it?
?
The Mu'tazila saw divine names as human inventions. In your own life, do you ever reduce God's attributes—like His mercy or justice—to mere concepts you can define, rather than realities that define you?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you brush it away with a ready answer, or do you wrestle until the truth truly settles in your heart? Which of these two answers would you have been tempted to give before reading this refutation?

The Third Answer: Pre-Eternity Through Signification
Presentation and critique of the third answer based on signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third answer that the names are pre-eternal because their signification (the meanings they refer to) is pre-eternal, and why this answer also fails.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity of signification reduces to pre-eternity of attributes, not names.

After two insufficient answers, a third proposal is presented: the divine names are pre-eternal because their signification (what they refer to) is pre-eternal. However, this answer faces the problem that the pre-eternity of the signification is nothing more than the pre-eternity of Allah's Essence and attributes, which does not directly address the pre-eternity of the names themselves. The author also notes it fails to refute the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic excerpt carefully
Read the Arabic excerpt, identifying the third answer and its critique.
2
Understand the reasoning
The names are pre-eternal because they signify pre-eternal meanings (e.g., Allah's attributes are pre-eternal). Therefore, the names are pre-eternal in terms of what they indicate.
3
Grasp the critique
The pre-eternity of the signification is derivative of the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes, so it does not establish a unique pre-eternity for the names as words or as designations. Moreover, it does not refute the Mu'tazila.
4
Compare with previous answers
How is this answer different from the first two? All three share the same pattern of redefining pre-eternity to avoid the issue.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it was answered that the meaning of its pre-eternity is that Allah is suitable for it eternally, so it is pre-eternal in consideration of suitability. But there is in this that it does not improve in refuting the Mu'tazila who say that they are from the creation of the creatures, since it does not contradict it. And some of them answered that its pre-eternity is from the perspective of Allah's knowledge and decree in pre-eternity, but there is in this that all contingent things are likewise. And it was said: its pre-eternity is from the perspective of its signification, but there is in this that the pre-eternity of the signification returns to what preceded of the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes, and it does not improve in refuting the Mu'tazila. And al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī transmitted from Sayyidī Muḥammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh al-ʿArabī: concerning what preceded, that from the pre-eternal speech of Allah are names for Him that are judged to be pre-eternal, just as from it are command and prohibition ... to the end. And upon this, what is intended by the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech upon the meanings of the names without division or fragmentation in speech, and this is what the heart opens to. And it is not objected that they did not mention from the notional divisions of speech the pre-eternal names, because their division is not exhaustive; rather they confined themselves to the most important in consideration of what appeared to them, how, and its signification is not encompassable?! The primacy: And al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī indicated at the end of his expression that pre-eternity here is not in the sense of non-existence but in the sense that they are placed before creation, so they are from His placing, exalted, before His creation, then He inspired them to the Muḥammadan light, then to the angels, then to the creation, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their saying that they are from the placement of humans, and in this speech there is an admission that the names are not eternal, as is not hidden. And in summary: this topic has not become clear.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a set of responses to the objection that the divine names are created. It begins by presenting an answer: the pre-eternity of the names means that Allah is eternally suitable for them, so they are pre-eternal in terms of suitability. However, this answer is criticized as insufficient against the Mu'tazila who claim the names are human inventions. A second answer says their pre-eternity is from Allah's eternal knowledge and decree, but this applies to all contingent things. A third says it is from their signification, but this reduces to the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes. The author then transmits a better answer from al-Mallawi: from Allah's eternal speech are names judged pre-eternal, just as commands and prohibitions are. Thus, the eternal naming means that speech eternally indicates the meanings of the names without division. This is the preferred answer. Al-Mallawi further clarifies that pre-eternity here means the names were placed by Allah before creation and then inspired to the Muhammadan light, angels, and creation, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The author concludes that this topic is not settled.

Key Terms
مدلول (madlul)
قدم (qidam)
ذات (dhat)
صفات (sifat)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a name signifies something pre-eternal, then the name itself is pre-eternal.
Correction: The pre-eternity of the signification is derivative; the names as words or concepts may still be created.

Checkpoint
What is the main critique of the third answer that the names are pre-eternal because of their signification?
A) It contradicts the Quran.
B) It does not apply to all names.
C) The pre-eternity of signification reduces to the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes, so it doesn't prove the names are pre-eternal.
D) It was not taught by the Prophet.

Key Takeaway
The third answer fails because it reduces the pre-eternity of names to the pre-eternity of their meanings, not independently establishing the names themselves as pre-eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah by His names, do you ever treat the name as a mere label without reflecting on the attribute it signifies? How can this lesson push you to deepen your connection to the actual attribute?
?
The third answer failed because it relied on a superficial link to pre-eternal meanings without providing new substance. In your own efforts to understand Allah, are you content with arguments that merely repeat what you already know, or do you press deeper? What concrete step will you take today to move beyond intellectual comfort?
